[{"featured_media": 0, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/leaving-the-stars-behind/", "date": "2016-05-18T06:52:45", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/www.vergemagazine.com/media/k2/items/cache/b235a32a656a9036c174f3ea74b1068e_XL.jpg?resize=609%2C406\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>This is a\u00a0hard post to write. It&#8217;s not technically complex, it doesn&#8217;t require some hidden knowledge to get right, but it&#8217;s the most human post I&#8217;ve written thus far, and because it cuts down to a really vulnerable part it&#8217;s difficult to get through. I\u00a0warn you now: it&#8217;s mostly stream-of-consciousness stuff so it&#8217;s not particularly well-written. If I name you and misrepresent you, I apologize. I also apologize if I meander a bit here and there. It&#8217;s been a long road to get here, and this is going to be a long post. Onward&#8230;</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<p>On March 24th, 2016 I left Astronomy, and it was by far the most difficult decision that I have ever made for myself to date. This was after 5.5 years at the graduate level, 3 years of undergrad Physics and Math building up to it, 1.5 years of undergrad business administration\u00a0prior to\u00a0that, and 1.5 years of fucking up at Binghamton University before that. I found my ambition and general zest for knowledge again through the lives and deaths of stars, and about a month and a half ago I left it behind. More than leaving behind the field, I left behind an identity; a high point of pride for myself and\u00a0my family, and damn if it isn&#8217;t one of the best decisions that I&#8217;ve ever made in my entire life. Let&#8217;s start at the start.</p>\n<p>I first fell in love with\u00a0the stars during the Spring semester of 2006. I was a Business Administration major at CUNY Queensborough Community College (QCC) intending to transfer onward to Baruch to become an accountant, and I\u00a0needed to take a science class for some sort of natural science credit. I didn&#8217;t want to work too hard at it so I chose Astronomy; it seemed easy enough and wow was it. No doubt my familiarity with Physics from high school helped but it was&#8230;yeah it was pretty simple. Even with that, as I learned about the stars and how they lived and died, along with the history of Astronomy and the larger concepts of our Universe, I fell more deeply in love with the Science. My professor at the time was <a href=\"http://www.qcc.cuny.edu/directory/FacultyDetail.aspx?personID=2048\">George Tremberger, Jr.</a>\u00a0and he picked up on my budding interest and asked me if I wanted to do some research over the Summer. One of the other professors had some grant money and could use a student to work with him doing some science. I had been planning to reach out to various accounting firms for an internship, but I was interested enough in the science that I took him up on it. That summer I used Excel (so young&#8230;so stupid) to read in some data from the Solar Heliospheric Observatory to try to detect fluctuations in the Sun&#8217;s light that could predict solar flares. My advisor was Dr. Tak Cheung and he was nice but&#8230;flawed. He wasn&#8217;t unique in that; he was the first in a string of flawed advisors that I&#8217;d have, but I&#8217;ll get there. He funded me but didn&#8217;t guide me much. That was tough, as\u00a0I had done all of one semester\u00a0of math-less intro-level astronomy. Regardless, I did some work, my results were inconclusive and I got to present\u00a0them at a local conference in NYC. Heh, I printed out my results on individual 8.5&#8243; x 11&#8243; sheets of paper and taped them to some poster board, Excel plots and all. Again, so young&#8230;</p>\n<p>I met a bunch of folks from the local NY astro scene at that conference and that was cool. I learned about an elective astronomy course at the American Museum of Natural History (AMNH) that was being taught by Charles Liu, friend and colleague to the illustrious Neil deGrasse Tyson, and I was encouraged to enroll so I did. Those classes were on Tuesday nights, so\u00a0every Tuesday evening in the Fall of 2006 I&#8217;d leave the QCC campus\u00a0after a full day of college classes and work as a math and accounting tutor to commute into the city.\u00a0For an hour and a half I&#8217;d ride the Q27, transfer to the 7 train, then again to the E train, walk through the bottom floor of AMNH to the lecture hall where I got a much better, much more fascinating introduction to stars living and dying. Supernova explosions! The conversion of mass directly into energy that became the light that made stars shine! The Big Bang! Black Holes! Holy shit this stuff was amazing, and I loved every damn minute of it. It was entirely worth the 1.5 hour trip in and the ~2.5 hour trip back out to Long Island every week. It was so interesting that it was a no brainer to sign up for the next semester&#8217;s course focusing on light and energy.</p>\n<p>Charles put me in touch with Timothy Paglione at CUNY York College, where I applied and got into the Physics and Math undergrad program. I had hoped to take some classes from him in astronomy and work with him to do\u00a0some research, but alas that wasn&#8217;t the case. Didn&#8217;t even get to take a Physics class from him. Boo! No matter, I had enrolled and damn if I wasn&#8217;t going to come out on top in the Physics program and put my best foot forward toward\u00a0astro. I started taking my first Physics class since SUNY Binghamton (which was god-awful and part of the reason I&#8217;d dropped out in the first place) and it was alright. The professor at the time wasn&#8217;t the best at conveying information, so I sought out the other professor teaching the same course across the hall, Gregory Boutis. He was awesome and taught me all my basic Physics. It was a great start and during the Fall semester that followed, pretty much until I left for Columbia&#8217;s bridge program\u00a0I worked in his lab doing not-Astronomy.</p>\n<p>The first real internship I got was in the summer of 2008, back at AMNH working with Charles. It was exciting because it would be\u00a0my first real step into the field, and in many ways it was a great introduction as to what to expect from my career in astronomy. A lot of awesome community amongst the students, very few people of color, and very little guidance from my advisor. I&#8217;m not gonna lie, that summer left me pretty pissed. Here I&#8217;d busted my ass to get into this program and I&#8217;d finally made it only to be left to flounder for half a summer. I had some support from the other students, and even a couple of the other advisors, but I was largely on my own trying to figure out what to do with myself. A huge fountain of support during that summer and onward even to this day was a student who&#8217;s been through it before, Amy Colon (now Gonzalez). When my advisor was nowhere to be found, she gave me the hands on guidance I needed. I&#8217;d had very little astro training and only a bit more physics training than that at the time, so I was just straight up lost more often than not. I ended up pulling something together and presenting at the end of the summer, but working without a clue\u00a0left a sour taste in my mouth. The other students did such great work and knew so much more than I did; I really started to question whether I belonged there at all.</p>\n<p>This trend continued in my next REU at the University of Wisconsin in 2009 where I looked\u00a0for X-ray sources in two dwarf galaxies. My advisor was a professor who had part time appointment there and in Italy, and she was somewhat hands off. I did a lot of re-inventing the wheel trying to figure out how to work with data from XMM-Newton, as well as trying to figure out what the hell I was supposed to be looking for in the first place, how to clean my data, how to interpret my data, what the hell an X-ray spectrum was and how to report hardness ratios, what a nova was, etc. Again, great support from the other students, some help from other faculty, not much guidance, sour taste. Great experience, but yeah a sour taste. If this sort of hands-off structure was the norm for astronomy, did I really belong? Would I really be able to make it in the field? A question for another time, because I was plowing ahead anyway.</p>\n<p>I went to a couple American Astronomical Society conferences along the way, presented my work, and met some people. In 2008 I met Marcel Agueros and he really looked out for me, pointing me toward the Columbia Bridge to the Ph.D. program in Astronomy for when I was ready to graduate. In 2009 I met some grad students\u00a0from the University of Washington and oh my god they were so awesome and laid back and accepting and\u00a0were 100% of the reason I chose to go there for graduate school. I also won a prize (yay!) for my poster on X-ray sources in the Fornax and Leo dwarf galaxies. Being at those conferences reinforced my feeling of community with the larger population of astronomers in and outside of the United States. The students were all great people doing great work, and everyone seemed willing to assist an up-and-coming student at all times. Still not many POC&#8217;s (but they did exist here and there!). Those were experiences I&#8217;d encourage any undergrad to go through.</p>\n<p>In 2009-2010 I finished my undergrad work at Columbia University as a part of the Bridge to the Ph.D. program, spending a lot of my time feeling extremely out of my depth. At York, all the physics classes beyond Physics 151 and 152 maxed out at like 8 students, so we all got a lot of attention. The profs made a strong effort to make sure that we got the material while still challenging us along the way. I appreciated that then and appreciate it still. This wasn&#8217;t the case\u00a0while I was at Columbia. Now, the Bridge program was great and taught me great things. I met great people and learned a ton. However, Quantum Mechanics destroyed me and was the first time I&#8217;d mentally checked out of Physics since 2003.\u00a0Radiative Processes in Astrophysics was less rough, but still had my eyes glazing over after a bit. It was the first time I&#8217;d felt really isolated in Physics/Astro; like I was so out of my depth because I didn&#8217;t belong. No matter, grit the teeth and bear it because there was a lot at stake and I couldn&#8217;t afford to flounder.</p>\n<p>My advisor while I was at Columbia was Jules Halpern (who also taught the Radiative Processes class). He was even more hands off than either of my previous advisors and I spent a lot of time where I should&#8217;ve been researching and learning to program just not knowing what the fuck I was doing. Just like&#8230;completely lost.\u00a0I had been hoping to at some point hit that Apprenticeship model that I&#8217;d had in my mind, where my advisor would spend a significant amount of time molding and shaping me into the type of researcher that could take on his legacy. I&#8217;d been hoping to find\u00a0it in my past few advisors as well and again came up disappointed. This became a common theme: disappointment at the lack of guidance. Disappointment in myself in not being able to conjure up my own direction and motivation. As a scientist you get trained to look for patterns and one was clearly emerging: my advisors thus far were varying degrees of absent. Maybe that&#8217;s just how they were? Maybe I wasn&#8217;t a compelling enough student to actually invest significant time in?</p>\n<p>When I talk about a lack of guidance and involvement, I don&#8217;t want to give the impression that I didn&#8217;t get any career-level guidance. The Bridge program was exceptional in that. Marcel took great care to make sure that we were able to sell ourselves extremely well on paper and through presentations. He stayed on our asses constantly to make sure we moved forward, and in those ways I flourished. However, my science work has\u00a0<em><strong>never</strong></em> experienced that level of involvement, so it didn&#8217;t grow in that same way. Anyway, onward to graduate school.</p>\n<p>Because of Marcel&#8217;s expert guidance in writing CVs, covers, and personal letters I was able to spin my isolation and mediocre science output due to lack of active mentorship into\u00a0proof of my ability to self-motivate and figure out my own path as a scientist. I figure that that and the research experiences I&#8217;d had up to that point are largely responsible for getting me into graduate schools I&#8217;d applied to. Still got some residual impostor syndrome going on there. I don&#8217;t know if\u00a0I&#8217;ll ever come to a point where I truly believe that my science skills were actually sufficient for the position that I got.</p>\n<p>I arrived in Seattle in late August 2010. It immediately felt different from NY where I&#8217;d up to that point spent my entire life. Everyone was so polite but impossible to build friendships with. The public transportation system was laughable. Everything closed at like 10pm. Oh, and there were like no black people. I think a little context is needed here. All through grade school I&#8217;d been in predominantly black and latino communities. QCC and York were also mostly black schools. Columbia was&#8230;quite free of melanin but it&#8217;s embedded in the Upper West Side of Harlem, which is still to some degree heavily melanated. So, moving to Seattle, where the demographics show between 6 and 8% blacks, was a huge culture shock. The University of Washington&#8217;s GO-MAP helped me survived, and later on in my grad career I built a community out of the POCs that went to their meetings. Capoeira definitely played a part in keeping my sanity, as it gave me instant community. The other astro grads also helped immensely; despite there being only one other black person in the department (including post-docs, faculty, and staff) I spent the majority of my social time during my first two years with the other astro grads.</p>\n<p>If it wasn&#8217;t for GO-MAP, capoeira, and the other grads I no doubt would&#8217;ve dropped first year. The other grad student I started working with on research was absent more often than not (working on thesis, traveling for stuff, other life things), and I was just drowning in school work. I managed to pull it through in most of my classes and worked\u00a0my ass off during second year along with the other grads in my cohort to pass the qualifying exam. Getting up to that point I had kept the notion in my mind that if I could just pass this exam it&#8217;d validate me being here. If I could just pass this exam, I wouldn&#8217;t be able to question whether or not I belonged. I passed the exam, but not the doubt.</p>\n<p>Throughout that time I had been doing research on red dwarf stars with Suzanne Hawley&#8217;s group. I had actually started with trying to work with Scott Anderson on some Sloan Digital Sky Survey data because he did high-energy astrophysics and at the time I was interested in that, but his style was hands off and he was also the department chair, so interactions were limited and not very fruitful for me. Suzanne on the other hand was (is) a beast. She was on all her undergrads, grad students, and post docs like white on rice. I particularly envied how well she kept up with her students professionally and personally, both past and present. I figured if I was gonna get the guidance I needed, she&#8217;d give it whether I wanted it her way or not (which is what I wanted). It worked out too; during second year the work that I&#8217;d done on the red dwarf AD Leo was shaped up for publication and pushed out (after many many revisions and much work). I had no idea what I&#8217;d do next but I figured if I could just be a part of her group I&#8217;d be solid.</p>\n<p>Unfortunately\u00a0when it was time for me to start thinking seriously about what thesis work I&#8217;d be doing and with who I&#8217;d be doing it with, she fell seriously ill. Then the year after she went on sabbatical, so that didn&#8217;t work out. I had however done some interesting work in my Galactic Structure class with the man who would become my advisor for the remainder of my grad school career, Zeljko Ivezic. He had us working on some new infrared data that we could use to map the entire sky much like it&#8217;d been done before with the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS; and those papers have been cited hundreds of times). He introduced me to Asymptotic Giant Branch stars, which are these red giant stars that are extremely bright and have some interesting internal and external physics. They pulsate, changing brightness by orders of magnitude, and they generate these dust clouds that make them ideal infrared targets. The work was truly interesting and I figured it had enough meat to it and possibility behind it where it could become a good thesis.</p>\n<p>Zeljko is a great guy and a fantastic scientist, but a horrible match for me as an advisor. I craved hands on guidance and regular, direct involvement. He&#8217;s a big name in the SDSS community, in other communities, and is the lead scientist for the telescope of the future, the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope (LSST), which will regularly capture petabytes of data and solve all the world&#8217;s problems. His various collaborations and responsibility to the community at large\u00a0make his time away from his office\u00a0on par with his time inside of it. Even without those commitments though, he&#8217;s not a real hands-on kind of guy (though he does start to turn the screws if progress is moving too slowly). So, when I started working for him,\u00a0it was pretty much immediately clear that it wouldn&#8217;t work out well for me. However I was interested in stars, and aside from Julianne Dalcanton, who already had her own legion\u00a0of\u00a0grad students, post-docs, research scientists, and her own international collaboration to manage with Hubble Space Telescope stuff, no one was really working on stars. So, I figured I&#8217;d grit my teeth again and just do what I could.\u00a0After all, I&#8217;d have to learn to be an independent scientist eventually, right? This could be seen\u00a0as just early prep. And so began\u00a0the next 3.5 years.</p>\n<p>There were other mitigating factors that led to my growing melancholy besides poor mentorship. Graduate students everywhere are <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/project/graduate-salaries-nationwide-interactive/\">notoriously poorly-paid</a>. When I started on in 2010 the salary was $18,300 per year, though it was funded through GO-MAP so I at least didn&#8217;t have to teach until the Spring quarter of 2011. After the qualifying exam, we got a small bump that amounted to about $100 more per month. I also started working on the planetarium as the technical coordinator, so that gave me 10% more each month\u00a0(which was quite nice) but still came under $22,000 per year. My third year was&#8230;slightly better-funded because I was on Zeljko&#8217;s grant, but at the end of that year the grant ran out and neither proposal we sent in afterward got funded. Without research funding, I had to TA the introductory astro classes and that was so emotionally draining. It&#8217;s hard to care and push forward when most of the 64 faces you see every other day contain nothing but apathy and contempt for being forced to take some stupid, irrelevant &#8220;natural worlds&#8221; credit. And then of course on top of having to grade mountains of paperwork every week I still had to try to push forward on research. In addition to that, and this is going to just sound petty, the constant drear of Seattle between the months of October and fucking July just wore on me. Being isolated from my family (including my brand new nephew)\u00a0and\u00a0lifelong friends (I visited a few times per year but not often enough), and having to manage a long-distance relationship (and the subsequent breakdown of the relationship when it became local in second year) just brought me down. Compound those with a criminal lack of diversity and the horrific\u00a0career prospects of\u00a0an astronomer. In grad school I finally got a clear picture of\u00a0what lie ahead: a\u00a0near-decade of post-doc purgatory, the gross undersupply\u00a0of faculty jobs, the grueling marathon of teaching, administrative, research, and mentoring work that comes with a faculty job, and the constant academic nomad status and work-life imbalance that came with all of that. I was climbing what first appeared\u00a0to be a small mountain but was actually the path to base camp at Mount Everest. Toward the end of my\u00a0third year, I started looking for an escape.</p>\n<p>One of my co-workers and eventual good friends, <a href=\"http://www.ifweassume.com/\">Jim Davenport</a>, would come around every so often and show me awesome data visualizations he had either seen or done himself. He inspired me to do my own, first as a means to learn my eventual programming language of choice\u2014glorious Python\u2014, and then just to explore personal projects of interest.\u00a0I did, and while I was involved in my own projects and blog posts I was, for those moments, free of feeling trapped in academia. Through pursuing my own projects I rediscovered my creativity, my ambition, and my passion for putting out meaningful work. It of course made the grad student experience that much worse, because I&#8217;d always have to come back to work that I was caring less and less about. Still, fulfillment lie in pulling on whatever thread my curiosity picked up, and so when I had off time I&#8217;d try some new shit. During this time, Data Science as a career seemed to just morph in out of the ether. I&#8217;d never heard of it before I&#8217;d entered grad school and it just seemed like a promise of a life that was too good to be true. Well compensated for curiosity and producing interesting work that relies heavily on data, analytical skill, creativity, aesthetic sense, and programming ability? What black magic was this? Is it even legal? How does it work? As I learned more about it and dove into more data projects, it increasingly seemed like the\u00a0yellow-brick road that would take me away from my deepening misery.\u00a0A couple of the senior grad students chose that path so it seemed more doable. Then my officemate Jake Vanderplas, Python and Statistics Guru, graduated and went on to become the director of UW&#8217;s\u00a0newly-constructed e-Science Institute. I wanted his brain and ability to make sense of damn near anything. I still do. When he ascended to his current throne, I started taking data science seriously.</p>\n<p>Another out, and the actual first domino to fall, came in during March of my fourth year (2014). I had been complaining to <a href=\"http://c.huntwalker.com/\">my brother</a>, as I often did, about how difficult it was to learn Python and MySQL on my own, as well as\u00a0how utterly broke\u00a0I was and how I&#8217;d love to have another stream of income. He owned his own business developing websites, and he\u00a0would tell\u00a0me how he&#8217;d pay me to work for him part time if I knew MySQL (I learned it by necessity for research) and would learn PHP, HTML, CSS, WordPress, JavaScript, and Photoshop. He&#8217;d brought this up to me several times prior to this, but I didn&#8217;t take him up on it because I figured there was no way I&#8217;d have the time to learn all that and still maintain sanity in grad school. However, leading up to this I&#8217;d also been gently pushing my lady at the time (now fianc\u00e9) to move across country and she finally agreed. That would take some dough to manage and between us we\u00a0had very little of it. Well, all that ability to teach myself shit due to absentee advisors since 2006 kicked my ass into gear, because I took him up on his offer. From March\u00a0to\u00a0June I spent 6am to 9am (or whenever I&#8217;d leave for school), as well as any free time I had after school, on weekends, and sometimes even during my workday teaching myself web development. The goal was to learn enough to do passable work, and I made it. Every day\u00a0between\u00a0June and late August I worked for my brother making $400 every two weeks building websites, waking up at 5:30 am and immediately starting work at 6 to sync up with his 9am on the East Coast. I&#8217;d work through to 10 am, then go off to campus where I&#8217;d stay\u00a0to about 6pm or so. Then I&#8217;d come home and continue working after hours on research if I needed to keep up my barest minimum, teaching stuff if I had things to grade, or more web dev practice. At the end of August I had to take a break because Zeljko sent me to Germany -&gt; Croatia -&gt; Italy for research (such a great experience).</p>\n<p>When I got back from my Eurotrip, I started back up\u00a0with my brother. Sure, the money was nice to have and was essential at the time. However,\u00a0aside from the money I flourished with the constant contact and immediate feedback. Doing good work made me feel good about myself, and the response was immediate so there was always a source for boosting my self-esteem after being beaten down by grad school for 4 years. Whether it was because this was his business and direct livelihood, or because I&#8217;m his brother, he gave a shit, and the difference was felt and appreciated every day. I&#8217;d work with him off and on as time would permit for the next year and a half.</p>\n<p>I loved having my work, and by extension myself, matter\u00a0so much that at the end of my fourth year I began seriously entertaining the idea of dropping out of grad school altogether. He would dissuade me many\u00a0times; he believed in the power of the paper that says Ph.D. He and my father would convince me that I would be wasting all the time I&#8217;d spent up to that point; that I was so close to finishing that it made sense to just keep gritting my teeth and white knuckling it through to the end. Aside from them though, I had to work on a few internal things. For one, I didn&#8217;t feel like my skills and credentials would be good enough for either a data science job (where they regularly reject\u00a0Ph.D&#8217;s) or a web developer position (I&#8217;d\u00a0<em><strong>just</strong></em> started learning it, and I was passable at best).\u00a0Second, I\u00a0<em><strong>was</strong></em> so close. I was steady working on my thesis proposal and on deck to go through my General Exam, which is the last gateway to the dissertation and those magic three letters. Maybe I should\u00a0just stick it out? Third, my identity was heavily coupled to astronomy. What would I be without it? Where could I go? What could I do? What use was I to the world if I wasn&#8217;t studying space, this thing I&#8217;d built myself up into doing over what was now about 8 years of time? Sure I was miserable, but so the fuck what? That was my problem to fix, and if I was a better astronomer I would probably not be so miserable. In order to leave, I&#8217;d have to leave my identity behind. I&#8217;d have to abandon\u00a0my astronomy support system, my international community, the bulk of my knowledge base. I&#8217;d have to abandon who I&#8217;d become up to that point. These points kept me struggling with whether or not to leave for almost another two years.</p>\n<p>As I fought myself on leaving, my misery deepened. I was quickly caring less and less about research, about my astro career, about anything that had to do with the science. I became aimless and deeply\u00a0unproductive, awakening and polishing up my code\u00a0whenever I knew a meeting with Zeljko was on the horizon. My only joy for my work came when my work was my own. When I was studying hip hop lyrics, or researching personal finance, or building D3 visualizations, or anything else that you see on this here site. February 2015 I fought through my general exam and came out of it triumphant, but just so totally drained.\u00a0I couldn&#8217;t give a first damn about any of it anymore. If I did work it was to satisfy some minimum. If I didn&#8217;t care, why press forward?</p>\n<p>I put my efforts into studying data science and looking for internships for that summer. That was an abject failure, but you&#8217;ve gotta start somewhere right?\u00a0The summer came and I didn&#8217;t have an internship so I taught again. When I had open nights I&#8217;d go to Python, JavaScript, and web development meet-ups trying to get my foot into the local community. Expenses were getting heavy due to the new living arrangement with the lady\u00a0so I also\u00a0worked for my brother, and because I wanted to be a better web developer I jumped deep into learning more about making dynamic sites in JavaScript using <a href=\"http://www.freecodecamp.com\">FreeCodeCamp</a>. At the time, FreeCodeCamp outsourced some of its lessons to sites like <a href=\"https://www.codeschool.com/\">Code School</a> and <a href=\"http://www.udacity.com\">Udacity</a>. Well, to help handle expenses I took out some student loans, and with the extra money I decided to take a shot in the dark and in October I enrolled in Udacity&#8217;s <a href=\"https://www.udacity.com/course/data-analyst-nanodegree--nd002\">nanodegree program in Data Analysis</a>. It was a small time commitment, an affordable financial commitment, and because I&#8217;d been a scientist for so long, a lot of the analysis work would be (and was) simple to execute. Around the same time, I&#8217;d been bitching on Twitter about my graduate school misery and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/IBJIYONGI\">Chanda Prescod-Weinstein</a>, a post-doc who&#8217;d come to UW to give a job talk, decided to take me under her wing and be my support system. She kept in touch with me regularly and put me in touch with other astronomers of color that she knew, as\u00a0she didn&#8217;t want to see another one of us fall through the cracks. I&#8217;m ever grateful. Julianne also stepped in and insisted that I meet with her regularly to progress steadily through research. Because of her urging, I made more progress than I would have on my own. But, I had other things weighing on my mind that needed dealing with.</p>\n<p>Through Chanda, I got in touch with <a href=\"https://twitter.com/astrojohnjohn\">John Asher Johnson</a>\u00a0who was generous enough to take like an hour out of his day to give me some career counseling. We had a very candid conversation, and I came out of it feeling like if I had to white knuckle it all the way to the Ph.D. finish, it may be worth my time to explore other avenues. More than that though was that it was okay to feel that way and do what&#8217;s best for me. Getting that sort of permission to do whatever I needed to do to get myself right was groundbreaking for me. A few weeks after talking to him, I started applying for jobs.</p>\n<p>From October 2015 through to this March I in some way, shape, or form applied to 35 companies in and out of Seattle. Out of those I landed interviews at 5 places, only one of which I had actually formally applied to. The other four I had gotten to the interview phase by some manner of personal connection, networking, or just sheer luck. I totally bombed my first interview too; I&#8217;d never done whiteboard work before. I&#8217;d been asked some questions about text manipulation and I&#8217;d only done brute force work up to that point. I&#8217;d also been asked some pretty advanced MySQL questions that I&#8217;d never even though to study, as I&#8217;d been using MySQL for years at that point and never needed more than just the basics. At that company I&#8217;d gotten two more interviews in a different department that I actually did pretty well at, but management had other ideas. I learned tons from my interviews at that company. I got my live question chops up, beefed up my projects from my Udacity Nanodegree program, and got a few more interviews. My projects sparkled on my resume and whenever I was asked to talk about them their quality came through. I eventually got to my interview at <a href=\"http://www.codefellows.com\">Code Fellows</a>. Through some manner of serendipity I managed to meet the Code Fellows COO at a fundraiser for the TAF Academy here in Seattle. He introduced me to the CEO who I had stupidly not paid much attention to at all (sorry Dave!), as well as the VP of Education who I ended up chatting with for quite a while. He encouraged me to send my info in, and they invited me for an interview. I arrived for that interview a week early because&#8230;reasons. It was on a Friday and I didn&#8217;t look hard enough at the date. A week after I went through that interview proper I came back to give a guest lecture for their Python dev accelerator class, talking about some of the many ways that we use Python in astronomy. The Monday following that lecture I was given an offer. The Thursday following that Monday I started work as an Assistant Instructor of Python.</p>\n<p>Since being at Code Fellows I&#8217;ve learned at light speed again. I&#8217;ve picked up a framework for building large-scale websites with Python, learned (and taught) about web app security, learned (and assisted students with) data structures, algorithms, workflows, project management mentalities, self-management mentalities, student management mentalities. I&#8217;ve learned that you can&#8217;t shoot students in the face with data science in a week without sufficient math background or else they&#8217;ll rebel. I&#8217;ve reacquainted myself with the value of consistent supervisor meetings. I now know what it&#8217;s like to feel like everything I do contributes to something greater that extends beyond just what advances my own career. I&#8217;m currently revising the Python curriculum for the classes to come, one of which I&#8217;ll hopefully be teaching full time, and I&#8217;m happy every day. Stressed for sure, and still working on the work-life balance thing, but I&#8217;m so damn happy. I have dreams about how I can contribute to the company. I&#8217;m optimistic about the future, about the classes I&#8217;ll be teaching, about how I can assist the students, and these are easily the best students I&#8217;ve ever taught. They&#8217;re so invested in the material; it&#8217;s refreshing to be around students who give at least as much of a shit as I do. They push themselves, and while they may complain about the deluge of work we&#8217;ve given them, they push us to challenge them daily. More than the management, the students motivate\u00a0me to become\u00a0the best instructor I can be, and I work on that daily. I look forward to when I&#8217;m directly shaping minds again myself (soon!)\u00a0and can give them all the guidance that I can squeeze out of my brain. Sure, it&#8217;s not data science, which was my intended path, but the organization is flexible and growing such that it&#8217;s not a far-fetched idea that I (we) could create a curriculum that can train junior data-literate software developers. Here, I see nothing but growth or the potential for growth.</p>\n<p>It&#8217;s interesting though. I&#8217;m so happy that every once in a while I have a hard time believing that it&#8217;s real. I still feel like I cheated the system\u2014that someone&#8217;s going to find out that I don&#8217;t actually belong where I am, doing what I&#8217;m doing, and that I&#8217;m going to have to go back to a life of inertia (hello again, Impostor Syndrome). It&#8217;s not that I don&#8217;t love the science of astronomy. I still want to learn about stars and galaxies and shit like that, HOWEVER I&#8217;ve been out a month and a half and I have zero desire to go back. Returning to that isn&#8217;t about the science for me. It&#8217;s about the misery and isolation associated with it. It&#8217;s about gritting my teeth again, being isolated again, being poor again, having to negotiate research meetings for a month from now, feeling shitty that I don&#8217;t do as much as others, feeling shitty about not being that high on my advisor&#8217;s radar, and having to lie to myself and others (friends, family, other grad students, faculty, randoms that can&#8217;t help but ask &#8220;so when are you going to finish?&#8221;) through a forced smile every single day about what I&#8217;m doing with my life.</p>\n<p>And so I&#8217;ve left the stars behind. There&#8217;s always going to be some guilt there, though far less than I&#8217;d expected. I still have to manage disappointment from family and acquaintances; I know dad really wanted to see me get that Ph.D. I&#8217;m sorry dad, but I can&#8217;t do that. To other black astronomers both present and future, I&#8217;m sorry I can&#8217;t join your ranks and decrease the isolation by one. I&#8217;m sorry I can&#8217;t be a role model for aspiring black Ph.D.&#8217;s. I&#8217;m sorry I can&#8217;t validate being included in Columbia&#8217;s Bridge program, or the efforts that every advisor both direct and indirect put forward into getting me as far as I got. I&#8217;m sorry other black Ph.D. students at UW that I&#8217;ve shared drinks and\u00a0dark thoughts about the\u00a0ongoing life-abuse that is grad school. I&#8217;m sorry, but I&#8217;m happy, and I&#8217;m going to take this as far as I can go.</p>\n<p>Cheers.</p>\n"}, "modified": "2016-05-18T07:04:09", "date_gmt": "2016-05-18T06:52:45", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=273", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=273", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/273"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/273/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=273"}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=273", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>This is a\u00a0hard post to write. I\u00a0warn you now: it&#8217;s mostly stream-of-consciousness stuff so it&#8217;s not particularly [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [41, 13, 35, 31, 26, 28], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=273"}, "id": 273, "title": {"rendered": "Leaving the Stars Behind"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2016-05-18T07:04:09", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "leaving-the-stars-behind", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 269, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/resolutionstargets-for-2016-pt-1/", "date": "2016-01-16T04:38:17", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p><img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-269\" src=\"http://i1.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/01/audit_action_plan_image-resized-600.png?resize=374%2C321\" alt=\"audit_action_plan_image-resized-600\" srcset=\"http://i1.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/01/audit_action_plan_image-resized-600.png?w=374 374w, http://i1.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/01/audit_action_plan_image-resized-600.png?resize=300%2C257 300w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 374px) 100vw, 374px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" />I want this to not just be a list of things that I want to do, as that&#8217;s as dismissible as any other list. After all, &#8220;A goal without a plan is just a wish&#8221; (Antoine de Saint-Exupery). As such, I want to identify the behavior that I want to introduce/change, and with it produce a realistic plan of action so that it can actually get done. If anything, it&#8217;ll end up resembling a list of actionable challenges. And I have quite a few, so this will just list the first few, and I&#8217;ll post the rest later.</p>\n<p>Let&#8217;s start with a basic list from which we flesh out the plans:</p>\n<ul class=\"ul1\">\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Begin reading comfortably in Portuguese</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Run half marathon</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Advanced level of fitness</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Better relationship with family</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Talk at NerdNite February 2016</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Start blogging again (even short ones)</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Read more books</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Start down the path of a statistician</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">More D3 data projects</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Have an engagement party</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Start planning a wedding in earnest</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">As many green boxes as is sensible</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Backflip? Backflip.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li1\"><span class=\"s2\">Finish the nanodegree</span></li>\n</ul>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\">I think that&#8217;s a good place to start. Let&#8217;s go through each, flesh it out beyond just a &#8220;wish&#8221;.</span></p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><b>Begin reading comfortably in Portuguese</b></span></p>\n<ul class=\"ul1\">\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The issue</b>: For a couple years now I&#8217;ve been using DuoLingo to keep my Portuguese knowledge up and current. However, the vocab list of DuoLingo is, while extensive, quite limited. It&#8217;s also very proper Portuguese instead of what&#8217;s more colloquial. What&#8217;s more, all the reading that I&#8217;ve done for the DuoLingo coursework is in short burts of individual words, or single phrases/sentences. So, I don&#8217;t have that good of a sense of how to read lengthy texts of portuguese.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The plan</b>: 2-3 times per week, use DuoLingo&#8217;s Immersion feature. I need ot make a habit out of it in order for it to stick (and for me to get better at it)</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>When I miss a day or two</b>: Just get back at it like I normally do when I miss a day or two (or five) of my regular Duolingo practice. Start on an article and just get into it.</span></li>\n</ul>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><b>Run a half marathon</b></span></p>\n<ul class=\"ul1\">\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The issue</b>: I promised a friend of mine that I&#8217;d do a half marathon with him in May, but I haven&#8217;t consistently run in about two months. It&#8217;s difficult to get motivation up at 6:30 am when all you want to do is sleep.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The plan</b>: I need to train up for it, and mornings are the only way to this. One thing that worked (once) was to think to myself &#8220;What would the better version of myself do right now?&#8221;. 30 minute runs in the morning, no matter the distance. I should also throw in a progressively longer run on weekends, as I need to train up to the 13.1 miles this will take from me. Again, mornings. Saturdays are best.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>When I miss a day or two</b>: Depends. If its a morning run missed, do leg exercises at home (squats, swings, lunges). If it&#8217;s a weekend run missed, then the next weekend don&#8217;t advance forward in time.</span></li>\n</ul>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><b>Advanced Level of Fitness</b></span></p>\n<ul class=\"ul1\">\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The issue</b>: Time is the great equalizer, and it&#8217;s slowing me down. I can feel it. And though I&#8217;ve had the benefit of some truly spectacular genes thus far keeping my body in shape (and let&#8217;s be honest, I move around a lot), I can&#8217;t continue to rely on my default fitness level for the rest of my life. The half-marathon is a short-term goal. In 4 months it&#8217;ll be done. I need to decide what type of fitness I want, and pursue that with consistency.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The plan</b>: I&#8217;ve realized that fitting in a set of bodyweight exercises and weightlifting activities is easy when I do it while doing other things.</span>\n<ul class=\"ul2\">\n<li class=\"li2\"><span class=\"s1\">Watching TV/movie? Exercise. Waiting for something to cook/sautee? Squats and lunges. Fitting exercise into the little spaces makes it that much more achievable.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><span class=\"s1\">There&#8217;s also Capoeira on Saturdays which I need to make consistent, and Body Pump on Sundays, which we need to get back to after this month is over (gotta avoid the resolutioners).</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><span class=\"s1\">As I restart/continue my morning runs, I cannot neglect the routine that I used to have, of knocking out pushups and pullups at that little part of Green Lake with the pull-up bar. I was making steady progress before. I can make it again.</span></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>When I miss a day or two</b>: The bodyweight and weight training stuff at home isn&#8217;t as consistent, so it&#8217;s not much of an issue. With everything else, it&#8217;s more about mindset than modifying my training regimen. I need to know that it&#8217;s ok that I missed a session, and that I can get right back on the horse the next day. I need to believe it to be true like I believe in the rising of the Sun.</span></li>\n</ul>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><b>Better Relationship with Family/Friends</b></span></p>\n<ul class=\"ul1\">\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The issue</b>: As with other things, I&#8217;m getting older, as is everyone else in my family. I&#8217;m turning 30 in April. My dad is turning 69, and my mom 61. As much as I hate to think about it, their time is limited, and everyone else&#8217;s time ticks forward as well. Their lives are all moving ahead and I&#8217;m not there to join them in that journey. It&#8217;s imperative that I get a better relationship with them, especially because I&#8217;m on the opposite coast.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The plan</b>: I already speak to my friends basically daily in group. What I need to do is routinely contact them individually. B calls me weekly, but I shouldn&#8217;t have to depend on him to call me to get in touch with him. I&#8217;ll start reaching out to each of them weekly just to catch up. D on weekends, as that&#8217;s when he&#8217;s most likely free. Everyone else can get a call on weekdays. As far as family goes, I have so much damn family that I&#8217;m going to just be texting most of them. I&#8217;ve already started a habit of texting Kayla more or less randomly. Need to text my other cousins too. Calling my mother is a lot easier now that she&#8217;s no longer teaching, and I need to call my dad when he&#8217;s home during the day so I can reach out to him and we can know each other better.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>When I miss a day or two</b>: Just pick a different day. It&#8217;s not that serious, as long as the effort is consistent (even if the days aren&#8217;t). Also don&#8217;t be afraid to text at odd hours. As long as they get a message.</span></li>\n</ul>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><b>Talk at NerdNite February 2016</b></span></p>\n<ul class=\"ul1\">\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The issue</b>: NerdNite February 2016 is in about a month and I&#8217;ve barely started my talk. And it&#8217;s going to take quite a bit of work.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The plan</b>: One night a week until the talk, it gets my full focus. The weekend before, it&#8217;s all I work on (unless something is truly urgent). I already have data of sorts for Yemanja and Oxum. I need all the rest. Focusing on one or more per week is doable. My best night to work on this sort of thing is either Wednesday or Thursday.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>When I miss a day or two</b>: If I miss a Wednesday, work on a Thursday. If I miss both, Saturday or Sunday.</span></li>\n</ul>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><b>Start blogging again (even short nonsense ones)</b></span></p>\n<ul class=\"ul1\">\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The issue</b>: It&#8217;s been more than 6 months since my last post of any kind. My site is basically derelict. I haven&#8217;t made any new projects and I haven&#8217;t written about anything interesting.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>The plan</b>: One post per month, whether it&#8217;s a blog post or a project post. A little writing every day will do wonders.</span></li>\n<li class=\"li2\"><b></b><span class=\"s1\"><b>When I miss a day or two</b>: Just get back on it the next day. As with most of my habits that I drop, I need to remind myself that it&#8217;s OK to lapse a bit. There&#8217;s no race to win, and there&#8217;s no consequence for stopping and starting. All that matters is that I do start again.</span></li>\n</ul>\n<p>I think that&#8217;s good enough for now. I&#8217;m still fleshing out the rest, so look forward to the next one! Hopefully these plans will help me accomplish these goals over the next 11.5 months. Till next time!</p>\n"}, "modified": "2016-01-16T04:39:07", "date_gmt": "2016-01-16T04:38:17", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=268", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=268", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/268"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/268/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/269", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=268", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=268"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>I want this to not just be a list of things that I want to do, as that&#8217;s as dismissible as any other list. After all, &#8220;A goal without a plan is just a wish&#8221; (Antoine de Saint-Exupery). As such, I want to identify the behavior that I want to introduce/change, and with it produce [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=268"}, "id": 268, "title": {"rendered": "Resolutions/Targets for 2016 \u2014 Pt. 1"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2016-01-16T04:39:07", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "resolutionstargets-for-2016-pt-1", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 250, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/education-in-washington-state-pt-1/", "date": "2015-03-20T14:41:36", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p><img class=\"aligncenter size-full\" src=\"http://i2.wp.com/www.rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/education.jpg?resize=750%2C422\" alt=\"education\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></p>\n<p>As an extension\u00a0of some work that I did for an acquaintance at Seattle&#8217;s <a href=\"http://www.roadmapproject.org/\" target=\"_blank\">Road Map Project</a>, I decided to look into some educational statistics for K-12 schools in\u00a0this great state of Washington. I was able to retrieve data from Washington State&#8217;s <a href=\"http://www.k12.wa.us/DataAdmin/default.aspx\" target=\"_blank\">Superintendent of Public Instruction</a>, which lists various Excel spreadsheets for educational statistics across the state, at the levels of state-wide, county-to-county, district-to-district, and school-to-school.\u00a0The entirety of my analysis with Python can be found <a href=\"http://nbviewer.ipython.org/github/nhuntwalker/RMP/blob/master/Road_Map_Project_Worksheet.ipynb\" target=\"_blank\">at this link</a>, but I&#8217;ll summarize what I can here.<br />\n<!--more-->The bottom line is that while there are some distressing trends, it doesn&#8217;t look all bad for Washington State, and certainly not for King County (where Seattle is located). The following map, for example, shows the estimated graduation rates in 2014 across the state, county-to-county.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_251\" style=\"width: 460px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/statewide_grad_rate.png\"><img class=\"wp-image-251 size-full\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/statewide_grad_rate.png?resize=460%2C288\" alt=\"statewide_grad_rate\" srcset=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/statewide_grad_rate.png?w=460 460w, http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/statewide_grad_rate.png?resize=300%2C188 300w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 460px) 100vw, 460px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">King County is outlined in red</figcaption></figure>\n<p>For reference, the average nationwide graduation rate\u00a0for the 2011-12 school year <a href=\"http://nces.ed.gov/programs/coe/indicator_coi.asp\" target=\"_blank\">was about 81%</a>. What this means is that, overall, Washington is performing at right around\u00a0the national average, give or take a few percentage points. Of course, there are some counties that are doing far better (e.g. Columbia &amp; Garfield counties; grad rates of 96.7% &amp; 96.4%), while some are in what appears to be a graduation crisis (e.g. Clallam &amp; Stevens counties; grad rates of 49.7% &amp; 64.7%). Further study may reveal some correlation of graduation rate with median household income, or another economic marker, but this is a start.</p>\n<p>We can, of course, also ask the question of how these counties have performed over time. A county may appear &#8220;good&#8221; now, but it may be on the decline if examined over a period of a few years. The following map looks into that question and shows the overall change in graduation rate over about 7 years.</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/differential_grad_rate.png\"><img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-252\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/differential_grad_rate.png?resize=460%2C288\" alt=\"differential_grad_rate\" srcset=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/differential_grad_rate.png?w=460 460w, http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/differential_grad_rate.png?resize=300%2C188 300w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 460px) 100vw, 460px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>This is a very promising map for Washington state, as it shows that over the past 7 years almost all counties have managed to improve on their graduation rates. Unfortunately, Stevens is down, as is Jefferson on the west side of the state, but most are seeing positive growth. Whatever initiatives have taken place over that seven year period are showing results!</p>\n<p>Now, graduation rates and changes over time may paint a positive picture of education in Washington state as a whole, but only one dimension of it. It&#8217;s taking all the results from all possible breakdowns of the data and flattening it, so we only see one side of a multi-faceted issue. The racial breakdown tells a different story.</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/grad_rate_2007_2014.png\"><img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-253\" src=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/grad_rate_2007_2014.png?resize=750%2C409\" alt=\"grad_rate_2007_2014\" srcset=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/grad_rate_2007_2014.png?w=792 792w, http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/grad_rate_2007_2014.png?resize=300%2C164 300w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 792px) 100vw, 792px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>This data makes one fact painfully clear:\u00a0<em><strong>for a K-12 student that isn&#8217;t white, the public school system is failing.</strong></em>\u00a0Note: I&#8217;ve excluded asians &amp; pacific islanders in this analysis as the 7-year data set combines them as one population for a few years, then separates them later on.\u00a0For blacks and hispanics, there is potential for a brighter future though rates appear to have stabilized within a few percent. First Nations students, however, aren&#8217;t making the cut. Their rates seem to fluctuate wildly, and appear to be trending downward since 2010. A closer look at the data shows low numbers, so they may be suffering from small changes in small numbers translating into larger percentage changes overall, but I&#8217;m willing to bet that it&#8217;s more than just a statistical difference. First Nations students are being particularly failed by the state of Washington, and I&#8217;d like to hear more about any programs targeting their a) retention, and b) mentoring/nurturing.</p>\n<p>That&#8217;s it for now. Next time we&#8217;ll look at some correlations between these data and some economic indicators, exploring how such economic factors can paint a fuller picture of what may influence educational outcomes for K-12 students.</p>\n"}, "modified": "2015-11-17T07:11:59", "date_gmt": "2015-03-20T14:41:36", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=249", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=249", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/249"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/249/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/250", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=249", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=249"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>As an extension\u00a0of some work that I did for an acquaintance at Seattle&#8217;s Road Map Project, I decided to look into some educational statistics for K-12 schools in\u00a0this great state of Washington. I was able to retrieve data from Washington State&#8217;s Superintendent of Public Instruction, which lists various Excel spreadsheets for educational statistics across the [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [61, 90, 2, 91, 49], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=249"}, "id": 249, "title": {"rendered": "Education in Washington State: Pt 1"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-11-17T07:11:59", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "education-in-washington-state-pt-1", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 28, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/love-marriage-and-taxes/", "date": "2015-01-02T05:03:31", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p><a href=\"http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-H3HHXPrEjF8/VKcyT9GUjHI/AAAAAAAAA_E/MqHR4_gX53U/s1600/taxman2.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-H3HHXPrEjF8/VKcyT9GUjHI/AAAAAAAAA_E/MqHR4_gX53U/s1600/taxman2.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"212\" border=\"0\" /></a>I started thinking about the future during this year&#8217;s winter break. What will I do after I get out of grad school? How much will I make? What type of life will I be able to sustain? I got to thinking about what type of salary I&#8217;d be able to command and what that&#8217;d look like each month. Then I realized that I hadn&#8217;t accurately accounted for taxes, or the effect of marriage. I looked up federal tax tables (<a href=\"http://www.forbes.com/sites/kellyphillipserb/2013/10/31/irs-announces-2014-tax-brackets-standard-deduction-amounts-and-more/\">here</a>) and realized it&#8217;d be a royal pain in the ass to try to calculate taxes for every individual salary I wanted to test, let alone accounting for marital status and whatnot. So, instead of doing each calculation individually, I decided to fire up Python and play with lots of numbers. If you want to see the calculations yourself, my python code is here (<a href=\"http://nbviewer.ipython.org/github/nhuntwalker/mystuff/blob/master/blog/interestPost/Fed%20and%20State%20Taxes%202014.ipynb\">link</a>). Here&#8217;s the results!</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<p><a href=\"http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-nVKMkv1ua-Y/VKN_PdO1hZI/AAAAAAAAA9s/xcwINxKFbEM/s1600/taxes_WANY.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-nVKMkv1ua-Y/VKN_PdO1hZI/AAAAAAAAA9s/xcwINxKFbEM/s1600/taxes_WANY.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"640\" height=\"211\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>I calculated the amount of taxes for salaries in various tax brackets. If you want to know more details about how things were calculated, follow the asterisk (*) to the bottom of the page. You can probably tell where each bracket begins/ends by the kinks in the lines. The blue lines represent single people, while the red lines represent married people. Solid lines are strictly calculating federal income tax, and would represent the total tax for people in these states: . \u00a0Dashed lines are federal and state income tax for people anywhere in New York State (except Yonkers and and the five boroughs), and dotted lines are specifically for people living in NYC. And in case you can&#8217;t read the axes here, the amount of money represented is the number on the axis times $1,000. Thus, if you&#8217;re single and making $80,000 per year, your federal income tax bill for 2014 should be about $20,000, and so on.</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-GuTFPUB7ezo/VKcwgwC4zjI/AAAAAAAAA-k/OpbW5NbILuM/s1600/marriage.jpg\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-GuTFPUB7ezo/VKcwgwC4zjI/AAAAAAAAA-k/OpbW5NbILuM/s1600/marriage.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"256\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>The first thing to notice here is that no matter where you live, being married carries with it a pretty sizable benefit (at least as far as paying taxes is concerned). And I didn&#8217;t even include the (tax) benefit of having a buttload of kids! So if you&#8217;re making $80,000 per year and dating let&#8217;s say an unemployed philosopher, put a ring on it and save yourselves about $6,000 per year (roughly $500 per month). <a href=\"http://nhuntwalker.blogspot.com/2014/02/long-term-investing-how-much-growth-is.html\">Hell, throw that extra money into an IRA</a>!</p>\n<p>The next obvious thing to notice is that to exist in the state or city of New York means to live with extra hands in your pockets (especially in the latter locale). Now why&#8217;d I pick New York to bully? Laziness and familiarity. I&#8217;m from there, so I&#8217;m somewhat interested in its economics, and I may end up back there in the end. Who knows? But hey, I&#8217;m in Washington state now, and we&#8217;ve got no city or state income tax so haha**! Anyway yeah, tax-wise, being married in New York city or state is about as bad as\u2014if not worse than\u2014being single <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_income_tax#States_with_no_individual_income_tax\">anywhere without income tax</a>. For example, let&#8217;s take a salary of about $80,000. In Washington state, that&#8217;d yield a tax bill of about $19,500 if you&#8217;re single, ~$14,000 if you&#8217;re married (and your <u>total</u>\u00a0household income was $80K). In New York state, that&#8217;s a tax bill of $23,750 and $17,500 respectively. In NYC, $26,500 and $20,250 respectively. You pay about $3-4,000 extra just for the privilege of living in NY state, and another $2.5-3,000 on top of that to experience city life. That&#8217;s a little cray cray.</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-ypknG-nqezE/VKcnt8nkzwI/AAAAAAAAA98/w9fLfnx4oKM/s1600/taxes_WANY_prct.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-ypknG-nqezE/VKcnt8nkzwI/AAAAAAAAA98/w9fLfnx4oKM/s1600/taxes_WANY_prct.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"640\" height=\"211\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-r2KlzgmXHG4/VKcw-Tr5TaI/AAAAAAAAA-w/PLobOmZcjMU/s1600/wave-slavery.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-r2KlzgmXHG4/VKcw-Tr5TaI/AAAAAAAAA-w/PLobOmZcjMU/s1600/wave-slavery.jpg\" alt=\"\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>The last not-so-obvious thing here though is that these taxes are detrimental to those that make the least. Above I&#8217;ve plotted the total percentage your taxes taken out of your gross income for the same income levels and circumstances above. Now let me explain my point: fed/state/city taxes are structured to adjust with your income, however living day-to-day is not. Whether you make $14,000 per year (roughly minimum wage) or $1.4 million per year, a 4-lb bag of frozen chicken breasts is still about $12 down at Safeway, and the bus/train/whatever still costs about $2.50 to use each time. Granted, if you&#8217;re making $1.4 million per year you&#8217;re probably not getting the $12 bag of chicken at Safeway. But, let&#8217;s scale up the impact it&#8217;d have on that salary. $12 chicken to someone that makes $14,000 per year is equal to about $1,200 for someone making $1.4 million. That&#8217;s some expensive chicken, even if you are a millionaire..</p>\n<p>So if you&#8217;re fresh out of undergrad or grad school, you&#8217;re single (as far as the IRS is concerned), and you live in NYC, I&#8217;m sorry. Cause that $50,000 starting salary is getting $13,700+ sucked out for taxes, and then you&#8217;ve still gotta pay rent (~$10,000 per year), buy food (please, eat out as little as possible, cause that might be another $10,000), get that metrocard, pay back those student loans, pay off that/those credit card(s), and still try to enjoy life. Better get yourself an inexpensive hobby, or hell maybe one that pays. There&#8217;s spots on subway platforms for every talent.</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-n7gw-13GIqc/VKcq13dWX2I/AAAAAAAAA-I/6yOoFMNWvnA/s1600/income_expenses_compare.png\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-n7gw-13GIqc/VKcq13dWX2I/AAAAAAAAA-I/6yOoFMNWvnA/s1600/income_expenses_compare.png\" alt=\"\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>I actually became more interested in what expenses would look like for someone making about that, so I figured it out. Above, we have the financial life of Mr. Johnny Averageface. He makes the <a href=\"http://www.census.gov/prod/2013pubs/acsbr12-02.pdf\">median American salary</a> ($50k; green), has average fun ($300/mo; blue), buys average food and eats out averagely ($150/wk; gray), <a href=\"http://www.finaid.org/calculators/loanpayments.phtml\">pays averagely</a> on his <a href=\"http://money.cnn.com/2013/12/04/pf/college/student-loan-debt/\">average student loans</a> ($345/mo on $30k of debt; orange), on his <a href=\"http://www.nerdwallet.com/blog/credit-card-data/average-credit-card-debt-household/\">average credit card debt</a> ($312/mo on $15k of debt; purple), on his average rent ($1,300/mo; yellow), and is taxed appropriately (red). Wow, rent is a bitch. But hey, taxes are giving rent a run for it&#8217;s money (well, Mr. Averageface&#8217;s money) in NYC.</p>\n<p>So what&#8217;s the takeaway(s) here? Get married, find a cheaper apartment, and don&#8217;t live in NYC. Also, not mentioned much above but for the hell of it here, <a href=\"http://nhuntwalker.blogspot.com/2014/02/long-term-investing-how-much-growth-is.html\">put your money in a goddamn IRA</a> already! Whatever you put in reduces your tax bill, gains interest (as long as the US economy doesn&#8217;t collapse), and gives you something to eat early-bird specials and live in south Florida with when you&#8217;re in your twilight years.</p>\n<div>\n<p>Happy New Year!</p>\n</div>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-wMBAntDcCnE/VKcxODmIGOI/AAAAAAAAA-4/PXuwZTvjLEg/s1600/Happy-New-Year-2015.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-wMBAntDcCnE/VKcxODmIGOI/AAAAAAAAA-4/PXuwZTvjLEg/s1600/Happy-New-Year-2015.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"400\" height=\"225\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<div>\n<p><i>Note, while the numbers quoted in text are approximated and rounded (because no one likes to see 12,736.04 instead of 12,700), the numbers in the figures are exact.</i></p>\n<p><i>* These are using the 2014 tax brackets for US federal, New York State, and New York City income tax. There really isn&#8217;t anything extra added. Just the standard deduction for each combination and one exemption. The taxes here include FICA and Medicare, and it only goes up to $200,000 in salary because Medicare is calculated in a weird way that I didn&#8217;t feel like figuring out after $200,000 ($250,000 for married couples).</i></p>\n<p>** <i>With that said, our public services pale in comparison to New York&#8217;s. It&#8217;d be like comparing a kitten to a 2-days hungry tiger in their ability and willingness to kill you.</i></p>\n</div>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-18T01:18:18", "date_gmt": "2015-01-02T05:03:31", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=27", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=27", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/27"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/27/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/28", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=27", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=27"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>I started thinking about the future during this year&#8217;s winter break. What will I do after I get out of grad school? How much will I make? What type of life will I be able to sustain? I got to thinking about what type of salary I&#8217;d be able to command and what that&#8217;d look [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [8, 9, 10, 11, 12], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=27"}, "id": 27, "title": {"rendered": "Love, Marriage, and Taxes"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-18T01:18:18", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "love-marriage-and-taxes", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 32, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/astro-questions-how-much-energy-is-needed-to-leave-the-galaxy/", "date": "2014-12-29T05:06:03", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p><a href=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/energyshutterstock_70986604.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-32 size-full\" src=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/energyshutterstock_70986604.jpg?resize=464%2C271\" alt=\"energyshutterstock_70986604\" srcset=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/energyshutterstock_70986604.jpg?w=464 464w, http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/energyshutterstock_70986604.jpg?resize=300%2C175 300w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 464px) 100vw, 464px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a>This is the third and final part to this thicker-than-you-think astro question of</p>\n<p><b><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><br />\n</span></b><b><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">Will we ever be able to travel outside the Milky Way Galaxy with either a manned or unmanned vehicle/device given the lifespan of humans and the restrictions on speed given by the speed of light?</span></b></p>\n<p><b><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><br />\n</span></b><span style=\"color: #333333; font-family: Arial, Tahoma, Helvetica, FreeSans, sans-serif;\"><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">You can find parts 1 and 2 <a href=\"http://nhuntwalker.blogspot.com/2014/11/astro-questions-difficulties-with-light.html\">here</a> and <a href=\"http://nhuntwalker.blogspot.com/2014/12/astro-questions-leaving-our-galaxy.html\">here</a>\u00a0(respectively) dealing with some of the difficulties of traveling at light speed, and then actually traversing the galaxy given its magnificent size. Let&#8217;s now think and talk about just how much energy we&#8217;d actually need. \u00a0\u00a0</span></span></p>\n<h3></h3>\n<h3 style=\"text-align: center;\">3. Blah blah, what&#8217;s all this about infinite energy? How much energy would we actually need?</h3>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<div>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Ix4fnWvsb2c/VKHHWyGAZ3I/AAAAAAAAA8c/HJhldH6Awqo/s1600/Hyperspace_falcon.png\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Ix4fnWvsb2c/VKHHWyGAZ3I/AAAAAAAAA8c/HJhldH6Awqo/s1600/Hyperspace_falcon.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"400\" height=\"170\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>I&#8217;ve clearly overlooked the need for energy thus far because it&#8217;s just damn troublesome. However, if you&#8217;re still reading, you might actually be interested in what that energy budget looks like. So let&#8217;s jump into this, with the knowledge that\u00a0<b>the short answer is <a href=\"http://www.urbandictionary.com/define.php?term=Metric+Fuckton\">a lot</a></b>. For this calculation, we need a mass. I&#8217;ll use myself as an example, as I (in my grand bias) feel that I&#8217;m about average height and weight for a healthy adult male. Last I checked, I&#8217;m 180 lbs\u2014so 81.8 kg. I&#8217;m going to accelerate myself to 40% light speed with an acceleration of 3g, then continue on at that speed forever. I&#8217;m choosing this speed because a) it&#8217;s damn fast, and b) it&#8217;s at about the threshold where relativity starts to really matter (the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lorentz_factor#Numerical_values\">Lorentz factor</a>\u00a0is what you have to multiply your mass by given some speed). The interesting thing here is that once I&#8217;ve accelerated up to the speed I want and I&#8217;ve left the Sun&#8217;s gravity (and am far from other sources of gravity), I really don&#8217;t\u00a0<i>need</i>\u00a0to expend much more energy. You can thank the\u00a0<a href=\"http://www.physicsclassroom.com/class/newtlaws/Lesson-1/Newton-s-First-Law\">law of inertia</a>\u00a0for that, as objects in motion stay in motion unless acted on by something else.</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-xOkUt2SVA10/VKHJDGtjAXI/AAAAAAAAA8o/Ayj2pCI0b8A/s1600/STS120LaunchHiRes-edit1.jpg\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-xOkUt2SVA10/VKHJDGtjAXI/AAAAAAAAA8o/Ayj2pCI0b8A/s1600/STS120LaunchHiRes-edit1.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"204\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>So the majority of my energy budget is spent in that first period of acceleration. I calculate this by figuring out how much\u00a0<a href=\"http://hyperphysics.phy-astr.gsu.edu/hbase/work.html\">work</a>\u00a0my\u00a0<i>massless</i><i>\u00a0</i>spaceship (basically no spaceship) needs to do (not accounting for relativity). The energy required for moving my mass <i>alone</i>\u00a0up to 40% light speed is about 590 quadrillion Joules, which means nothing to you so let&#8217;s make it more relatable. That amount of energy is about the same amount consumed by\u00a0<a href=\"http://www.eia.gov/state/seds/data.cfm?incfile=/state/seds/sep_fuel/html/fuel_te.html&amp;sid=US\">the entire state of North Dakota in 2012</a>. But hey, like\u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Dakota#Population\">no one lives in North Dakota</a>. And, that&#8217;s JUST my mass\u2014I&#8217;d need to be in a shuttle. The now-retired space shuttle\u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle\">weighs about 2,030 tons</a>, most of which is the weight of the fuel. I&#8217;m not going to bother adding my mass to that. That&#8217;s just stupid, like considering the effect of the weight of an individual grain of dust on your own weight. Anyway, the shuttle (including the thrusters) weighs that much and only accelerates for about 600 seconds (or 10 minutes). Let&#8217;s do the entirely wrong thing and directly scale up the weight of the shuttle by the time needed to go through the whole acceleration period we calculated earlier: 47 days or 4.06 million seconds. That&#8217;s a factor of about 6,800! So you&#8217;d probably have to increase the mass of your shuttle to a number around 12.5 billion kg!!!</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-tapuqCApxdM/VKHJr_ddyOI/AAAAAAAAA8w/CpzLVKQ9ygw/s1600/Sts_et_cutaway.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-tapuqCApxdM/VKHJr_ddyOI/AAAAAAAAA8w/CpzLVKQ9ygw/s1600/Sts_et_cutaway.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"640\" height=\"256\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p><b>Note </b>that this is not at all the definitive answer of how heavy the new shuttle would be, just a poor ballpark figure. There&#8217;s a host of other things to keep in mind here. For example, with every pound of fuel you add into your shuttle to push it some extra distance, you have to also include enough fuel to push that extra pound. You have to also include more metal to make a bigger fuel tank, and then include enough fuel to account for that extra metal. You have to also consider that we eventually drop fuel tanks once they&#8217;ve been exhausted, and that we always include a bit more fuel than is actually needed. As you can see, it&#8217;s a little more complicated than simply scaling things.
