However, I don&#8217;t want to do all that so I&#8217;m just going with the na\u00efve base answer, and am\u00a0<a href=\"http://assets.diylol.com/hfs/5df/6e7/e54/resized/engineering-professor-meme-generator-the-proof-is-left-as-an-exercise-for-the-reader-359485.jpg\">leaving it as an exercise for the reader</a> to figure out how to do it more precisely.</p>\n<p>Ignoring the fact that that new weight is entirely impractical and wrong, the energy needed to get that shuttle mass up to 40% light speed over 47 days is now not 590 quadrillion Joules, but about 90 trillion trillion Joules (we&#8217;re talking nonsense numbers here).\u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_energy_consumption\">In 2008,\u00a0<i>the entire world</i>\u00a0consumed not even a fraction of a fraction of that</a>. In fact, you&#8217;d have to\u00a0<b>take that rate of worldwide consumption and extend it over about 174,000 years</b>\u00a0in order to equal the amount of energy it&#8217;d take to get that shuttle up to 40% light speed,\u00a0<i>and we&#8217;re not even including relativity!\u00a0</i>Basically it&#8217;d take more energy than humanity&#8217;s\u00a0<i><b>ever</b></i>\u00a0consumed! What&#8217;s the next thing that can compare to the energy needed? All of the output of\u00a0<i><a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun\">THE GODDAMN SUN</a>\u00a0</i>in every direction\u00a0for ONE SECOND, which is more than 4 times our energy budget.</p>\n<p>Did you hear that? That was the sound of your mind being blown.</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-45OFf30j1tQ/VKHLHzvVUZI/AAAAAAAAA88/E6tHyxiIEjQ/s1600/mindblown.gif\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-45OFf30j1tQ/VKHLHzvVUZI/AAAAAAAAA88/E6tHyxiIEjQ/s1600/mindblown.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"180\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>You&#8217;re welcome.</p>\n</div>\n<div></div>\n<div>\n<p><i>Keep in mind that these calculations are done on the fly and are often rounded. Check the links for more detail and keep in mind that I cut out relativity a lot because it complicates the hell out of things.</i></p>\n</div>\n<div></div>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-18T01:18:01", "date_gmt": "2014-12-29T05:06:03", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=31", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=31", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/31"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/31/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/32", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=31", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=31"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>This is the third and final part to this thicker-than-you-think astro question of Will we ever be able to travel outside the Milky Way Galaxy with either a manned or unmanned vehicle/device given the lifespan of humans and the restrictions on speed given by the speed of light? You can find parts 1 and 2 [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [13, 14, 15, 16, 17, 18, 19], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=31"}, "id": 31, "title": {"rendered": "Astro Questions: How Much Energy is Needed to Leave the Galaxy?"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-18T01:18:01", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "astro-questions-how-much-energy-is-needed-to-leave-the-galaxy", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 229, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/the-price-of-pie/", "date": "2014-12-21T02:39:25", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-Vr64x6D8FIY/VJbwFtLcmKI/AAAAAAAAA7s/eyvf6Mqs3Ps/s1600/piestand.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-Vr64x6D8FIY/VJbwFtLcmKI/AAAAAAAAA7s/eyvf6Mqs3Ps/s1600/piestand.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>I woke up this morning after having a dream that my future kids (no doubt a little more brown than above) opened up a stand where they sold apple pies. They would set up the stand on a busy street corner, sell the pie in slices for some amount per slice, be wildly successful, and they&#8217;d get all their spending money off of that business. When I woke up, I found myself wondering if that was actually doable. Or rather, <b>how much would it cost to sell an apple pie?</b></p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<p>Everything that follows is based on the recipe found here:\u00a0<a href=\"http://www.epicurious.com/recipes/food/views/Cinnamon-Crumble-Apple-Pie-108650\">http://www.epicurious.com/recipes/food/views/Cinnamon-Crumble-Apple-Pie-108650</a>, the prices of things at Safeway (get those sales boy!), and the sales tax rate in Seattle (currently 9.5%). I&#8217;m not including the costs of actually driving to get the ingredients, the electricity/gas it takes to cook the pies, or other things that are just available at home like water. I&#8217;m also not getting fancy with it and including gluten-free anything or whole wheat flour or whatever else. This is just basic-ass homemade apple pie from scratch.</p>\n<p>Going down the ingredients list, we have totals of&#8230;</p>\n<ul>\n<li>2.5 cups flour</li>\n<li>1.5 tsp salt</li>\n<li>1.2 cups sugar</li>\n<li>0.25 cups brown sugar</li>\n<li>10 tbsp unsalted butter</li>\n<li>0.25 cups vegetable shortening</li>\n<li>0.5 tsp apple cider vinegar</li>\n<li>3.25 pounds apples</li>\n<li>3.5 tsp cinnamon</li>\n<li>0.5 tsp vanilla extract (I&#8217;m adding this one in)</li>\n</ul>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-43zw90WauWU/VJbwuVo8HqI/AAAAAAAAA70/KzFNDxyF6zs/s1600/Swedish-Apple-Pie-Ingredients.jpg\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-43zw90WauWU/VJbwuVo8HqI/AAAAAAAAA70/KzFNDxyF6zs/s1600/Swedish-Apple-Pie-Ingredients.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"214\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>Now of course, you can&#8217;t just head down to Safeway and buy 1.5 teaspoons of salt, or one and a quarter sticks of butter. You&#8217;ve gotta buy the whole package of everything. Thus, the price list looks like</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Flour &#8211; $1.88 for 5 lbs</li>\n<li>Sugar &#8211; $1.99 for 4 lbs</li>\n<li>Apples &#8211; $1.99 per pound</li>\n<li>Brown Sugar &#8211; $5 for 6 lbs</li>\n<li>Unsalted sweet cream butter &#8211; $3.99 per pound</li>\n<li>Salt &#8211; $0.69 for 26 oz</li>\n<li>Shortening &#8211; $5.49 for 48 oz</li>\n<li>Apple Cider Vinegar &#8211; $6.19 for 128 fl oz</li>\n<li>Vanilla Extract &#8211; $8.29 for 4 fl oz</li>\n<li>Cinnamon &#8211; $9.78 for 12.36 oz</li>\n</ul>\n<div>\n<p>The assumption here is that you&#8217;re gonna make a number of pies, instead of just one. So it makes more sense to buy in bigger quantities to get the best price per whatever unit weight/volume each item has. So you go, you do this, and <b>that first trip subtotals to $49.80, with 9.5% sales tax knocking it up to $54.53</b>. Hot damn! You damn sure better make more than one pie at that rate. \u00a0Breaking it down, each part of the pie is costing you&#8230;</p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<ul>\n<li>Sugar &#8211; $0.27 (0.53 lbs)</li>\n<li>Flour &#8211; $0.27 (0.69 lbs)</li>\n<li>Apples &#8211; $6.50 (3.25 lbs)</li>\n<li>Brown Sugar &#8211; $0.10 (0.11 lbs)</li>\n<li>Butter &#8211; $2.50 (0.8 lbs)</li>\n<li>Cinnamon &#8211; $0.26 (0.32 oz)</li>\n<li>Vanilla &#8211; $0.18 (0.083 fl oz)</li>\n<li>AC Vinegar &#8211; $0.01 (0.083 fl oz)</li>\n<li>Salt &#8211; $0.01 (0.3 oz)</li>\n<li>Shortening &#8211; $0.21 (1.8125 oz)</li>\n</ul>\n<p><b>This comes up to about $10.31 per pie, $11.29 after sales tax</b>. Yeesh, there goes the dream of selling them at a dollar per slice. Good god, if only those apples didn&#8217;t cost so much. Like, if you had an apple tree in your back yard you could make a killing on these things (provided you don&#8217;t run out of apples).</p>\n</div>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-w70o1_N55Hw/VJbxNYfg6kI/AAAAAAAAA78/vqLXNoloJLM/s1600/apple-orchard-photograph-by-jeff-kubina-flickr.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-w70o1_N55Hw/VJbxNYfg6kI/AAAAAAAAA78/vqLXNoloJLM/s1600/apple-orchard-photograph-by-jeff-kubina-flickr.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"211\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<div>\u00a0Getting this far raises two more questions in my mind: <b>how many pies can we make before running out of our first (non-apple) ingredient? </b>and\u00a0<b>what&#8217;s the price per slice to make it worthwhile?</b></div>\n<div>\n<p>In Chemistry there&#8217;s the idea of <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limiting_reagent\">the limiting reagent</a>, where your chemical reaction is limited by whatever ingredient gets used the fastest. What&#8217;s our limiting reagent here, aside from apples? The next thing to go before the next grocery store trip is clearly butter, since one run to the grocery store will only bring back enough apples and butter to make one pie. That, my friends, would be a waste of all those other ingredients. Let&#8217;s just say we buy however many bags of apples and boxes of butter we&#8217;d need. So what&#8217;s next? It&#8217;s a close race, but the flour actually goes just before the sugar does. <b>You could make about 7 pies</b> with that amount of flour (7.25 pies really, but who&#8217;s making a quarter pie?). To buy all the apples and butter necessary and all the ingredients for the first run, <b>it&#8217;d cost you about $119.10 </b>including tax.</p>\n</div>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-Tl7KbKQVhSw/VJbxerAYKUI/AAAAAAAAA8E/l2kVnNkyW6k/s1600/o-KIDS-AND-MONEY-facebook.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-Tl7KbKQVhSw/VJbxerAYKUI/AAAAAAAAA8E/l2kVnNkyW6k/s1600/o-KIDS-AND-MONEY-facebook.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"213\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<div>\n<p>So we make our 7 pies and now we sell them by the slice\u20148 slices per pie for 7 pies making 56 slices. \u00a0In order to break even, we&#8217;d have to sell your pies at about $2 per slice. Actually, I&#8217;m rounding; selling at $2 per slice puts you at a net loss of $7.10. That won&#8217;t do. I don&#8217;t know about you, but I&#8217;d like to be able to use this to teach my kids about business, and what&#8217;s a business that doesn&#8217;t make a profit worth talking about? They&#8217;re kids so they would be happy making even $5 per hour, and let&#8217;s say we sell for an afternoon for 4 hours if I could get them to sit still and be dedicated for that long. This rounds out to $20 profit (a 16.8% gain). <b>That&#8217;d require the pies to be like $2.50 per slice at $20 per pie.</b></p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<p>Honestly, that&#8217;s not a terrible haul. And in the meantime they&#8217;d learn a little about business and a lot about the value of hard work. Something to think about.</p>\n</div>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-19T02:42:18", "date_gmt": "2014-12-21T02:39:25", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=228", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=228", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/228"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/228/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/229", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=228", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=228"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>I woke up this morning after having a dream that my future kids (no doubt a little more brown than above) opened up a stand where they sold apple pies. They would set up the stand on a busy street corner, sell the pie in slices for some amount per slice, be wildly successful, and [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [84, 85, 86, 87, 10, 88, 89], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=228"}, "id": 228, "title": {"rendered": "The Price of Pie"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-19T02:42:18", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "the-price-of-pie", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 226, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/astro-questions-leaving-our-galaxy/", "date": "2014-12-17T02:33:12", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-OEXHtlUoWLo/VGJM4KOZeyI/AAAAAAAAA4c/s8pnvzIfGtU/s1600/black-hole-milky-way.jpg\"><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-OEXHtlUoWLo/VGJM4KOZeyI/AAAAAAAAA4c/s8pnvzIfGtU/s1600/black-hole-milky-way.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /></span></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">So this post is a continuation of the last major astro question:<br />\n</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><br />\n</span><b><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">Will we ever be able to travel outside the Milky Way Galaxy with either a manned or unmanned vehicle/device given the lifespan of humans and the restrictions on speed given by the speed of light?<br />\n</span></b><b><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><br />\n</span></b><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">I addressed <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/astro-questions-difficulties-with-light-speed/\">the first part of the question</a>, about the difficulties of traveling at light speed. Now, I want to start with the assumption that we can already reach light speed, and talk about just how damn large our Milky Way galaxy is. So, let&#8217;s begin:</span></p>\n<h3 style=\"text-align: center;\"><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">2. Traveling at light speed, would we be able to travel outside the Milky Way with either manned or unmanned spacecraft?</span></h3>\n<div>\n<p><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><!--more--><br />\nSo now, by some means we&#8217;ve accelerated up to our speed of light, and we want to travel outside\u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way\">the Milky Way</a>. The short answer is\u00a0<b>no, we wouldn&#8217;t be able to travel outside the Milky Way,\u00a0</b>but let&#8217;s look at this in stages. First we have to leave the Solar System. We characterize the size of the Solar System by where the Sun&#8217;s gravity no longer dominates. This is about 1-2 light years away from the Sun; let&#8217;s say 1 light year. So, 1 full year at light speed to leave the Solar System. That&#8217;s a start.<br />\n</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><br />\n</span><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-RtduM5PXd0g/VJIPN_JzIfI/AAAAAAAAA7A/eY5moBKL-Bk/s1600/galaxy-schematic.jpg\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-RtduM5PXd0g/VJIPN_JzIfI/AAAAAAAAA7A/eY5moBKL-Bk/s1600/galaxy-schematic.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"242\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /></a><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">Now the Milky Way itself has a decent bit of structure, where the disk of the Milky Way (i.e. where most of the stars, gas, and dust are) has a thin component (~ 500 parsecs or 1,631 light years), and a thick component (~ 1,000 parsecs or 3,262 light years). The Milky Way is more than just the disk though, with a large, roughly-spherical distribution of older stars (and dark matter) around it known as\u00a0<a href=\"http://csep10.phys.utk.edu/astr162/lect/milkyway/components.html\">the galactic halo</a>. The size of this halo isn&#8217;t&#8230;well-defined. It&#8217;s got a lot of <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Globular_cluster\">clusters of stars</a> though, so we can define its &#8220;edge&#8221; by where the overwhelming majority (90%) of these clusters are contained. This is about 100,000 light years away from the Sun. So, at the speed of light it&#8217;d take us about 1 year to escape the Sun, ~1,600 years to leave the thin disk, ~3,300 years to leave the thick disk, and 100,000 light years to reach the edge of the Milky Way. No human would survive any one of these trips (save for the trip out of the Solar System), not even\u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methuselah\">Methusaleh</a>.<br />\n</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><br />\n</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">Let&#8217;s get a little bit more practical, since as far as we know\u00a0<b>it&#8217;s impossible for anything with mass to reach light speed</b>. The effects of relativity start to become significant at about 40% light speed (specifically meaning that the kinetic energy of something moving at 40% light speed is 9% higher than it would be without relativity). So, moving constantly at 40% light speed from Earth, it&#8217;d take about 250,000 years for a spacecraft to reach the edge of the Milky Way. These are big numbers, so let&#8217;s give a reference that might be more understandable.\u00a0<a href=\"http://humanorigins.si.edu/evidence/human-evolution-timeline-interactive\">Anatomically-modern humans</a>\u00a0(homo sapiens sapiens) evolved in Africa roughly 200,000 years ago. That means that\u00a0<b>it&#8217;d take more time to leave the Milky Way at a &#8220;reasonable&#8221; speed than humans have even existed.</b>\u00a0And we&#8217;re not even talking about the building of a spacecraft that could support a viable offshoot of the human race! Let&#8217;s modify this even more.<i></i></span></p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-NpZ2KiFpyo4/VJIPjWLxwDI/AAAAAAAAA7I/7WUDcxJ09a0/s1600/evolution_timeline.jpeg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-NpZ2KiFpyo4/VJIPjWLxwDI/AAAAAAAAA7I/7WUDcxJ09a0/s1600/evolution_timeline.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"640\" height=\"228\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><br />\n</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">Now, you know and I know that you don&#8217;t just start moving at high speeds from rest. You have to accelerate up to it. <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/astro-questions-difficulties-with-light-speed/\">We&#8217;ve already established</a> that we could probably withstand an acceleration of 3g indefinitely. Thus, with infinite energy at our disposal we accelerate from rest up to 40% light speed, and then maintain that speed&#8230;basically forever. It&#8217;d take about 47 days of heavy discomfort to get up to 40% light speed, over which you&#8217;d travel about 1600 times the distance between the Earth and the Sun. After that point it turns out that the timescales are still more or less the same as before, since the distance covered at this point is nothing compared to the distance that would still need to be covered.<br />\n</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\"><br />\n</span><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">Long story short? Even at light speed, hell even at many times light speed, no one person would ever survive the trip out of the galaxy. You&#8217;d have to have a fully self-sustaining society and system of resources for that society to live just to get a picture of the disk. Long story even shorter? <b>The Milky Way galaxy is really freaking big.</p>\n<p></b></span><i>Keep in mind that these calculations are done on the fly and are often rounded. Check the links for more detail and keep in mind that I cut out relativity a lot because it complicates the hell out of things.</i></p>\n</div>\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-19T02:37:38", "date_gmt": "2014-12-17T02:33:12", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=225", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=225", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/225"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/225/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/226", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=225", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=225"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>So this post is a continuation of the last major astro question: Will we ever be able to travel outside the Milky Way Galaxy with either a manned or unmanned vehicle/device given the lifespan of humans and the restrictions on speed given by the speed of light? I addressed the first part of the question, [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [13, 83, 80, 16, 18], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=225"}, "id": 225, "title": {"rendered": "Astro Questions: Leaving our Galaxy"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-19T02:37:38", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "astro-questions-leaving-our-galaxy", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 223, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/astro-questions-difficulties-with-light-speed/", "date": "2014-11-30T02:28:55", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p><a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-2UQqibAX7DE/VHkEPlpJTNI/AAAAAAAAA5s/H8WnyBDqcqg/s1600/andromeda_banner.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-2UQqibAX7DE/VHkEPlpJTNI/AAAAAAAAA5s/H8WnyBDqcqg/s1600/andromeda_banner.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"400\" height=\"225\" border=\"0\" /></a>This academic quarter, I did something a little different than I normally do. In addition to simply assigning and grading labs as was required, I made an assignment of my own that my students were responsible for each week. They had the option of:</p>\n<p>\u2022 Finding an article about astronomy and writing a page about it</p>\n<p>\u2022 Finding an astronomical concept they found interesting and write a page about that</p>\n<p>\u2022 Submit a question about physics or astronomy which I would answer and return to them.</p>\n<div>\n<p>I figured most students would just take the easy route and submit questions, which could be of any length, and many did. However, the questions asked weren&#8217;t simple! Or perhaps the students thought they were simple going in, but it ended up being quite the opposite when answered. I got a kick out of answering these questions each week so much so that I decided to collect some of the most interesting questions I&#8217;ve been asked and answer them* again right here.</p>\n<p><!--more--><br />\nThis entry&#8217;s answered question is perhaps a little more involved than the student originally intended:</p>\n</div>\n<blockquote class=\"tr_bq\"><p><b>Will we ever be able to travel outside the Milky Way Galaxy with either a manned or unmanned vehicle/device given the lifespan of humans and the restrictions on speed given by the speed of light?</b></p></blockquote>\n<div>\n<p>The way I see it, this question is actually a few questions rolled into one. So, I&#8217;ll answer them in separate posts. This post will hit the first one.</p>\n</div>\n<h3 style=\"text-align: center;\">1. What are some of the limitations of traveling at the speed of light?</h3>\n<div>\n<figure style=\"width: 320px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-wriaJIWsVgM/VHwHeKdee0I/AAAAAAAAA58/KbeWUKgDIls/s1600/light_speed.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"175\" border=\"0\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Light Speeeeeeeeeed!</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Let&#8217;s start this section by saying that, as far as we currently know,\u00a0<b>traveling at the speed of light is only possible for massless particles (like photons)</b>. This is all because of\u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_relativity\">relativity</a>, and the fact that the faster something moves, the more energy it takes to continue to accelerate that particle. This is especially apparent as you approach the speed of light, where &#8220;small&#8221; changes in velocity require near-infinite inputs of energy. Eventually you get to a point where the energy required to push that mass faster is laughably high, such that\u00a0<b>it might as well be infinite</b>.</p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<figure style=\"width: 237px\" class=\"wp-caption alignleft\"><img src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-yVS22_TWjZo/VHwH1tekkRI/AAAAAAAAA6E/jq5622FR1Og/s1600/tons_tnt.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"237\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">1,750 tons TNT</figcaption></figure>\n<p>This is all very conceptual, so let&#8217;s have an example. I want to have a 1-gram cube of sugar move at 10% of light speed (with respect to my position on Earth). Without taking relativity into account, and assuming we&#8217;re not accelerating it up from rest, I just calculate the\u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kinetic_energy\">kinetic energy</a>\u00a0(energy of an object in motion) of a 1-gram object moving at that speed. That kinetic energy happens to be about 450 billion Joules. For reference,\u00a0<b>that&#8217;s about the energy released in detonating one hundred and seven and a half tons of TNT.</b>\u00a0Now, let&#8217;s account for relativity. Relativity is special in that\u00a0<b>when a mass is moving, its velocity modifies its mass.</b>\u00a0Thus, instead of its kinetic energy being 450 billion Joules, its actually closer to 452.3 billion Joules. That doesn&#8217;t sound like a lot, but it is adding another half ton of TNT onto the pile. Not very impressive? Let&#8217;s see the difference at 99% of light speed.</p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<p>At 99% light speed, the kinetic energy of the 1-gram cube of sugar would be about 44.1 trillion Joules, or 10.5 kilotons (kilo = thousand) of TNT. Accounting for relativity we increase the mass by a factor of 7, and thus the energy by a factor of 7. Now we&#8217;re going from 10.5 kilotons to 74.7 kilotons of TNT in energy\u2014or about three and a half of the atomic bomb dropped on Nagasaki in WW2. And as you get ever closer to the speed of light (99.9%, 99.99%, 99.999%, etc), the kinetic energy of that 1-gram cube of sugar also increases (240 kilotons TNT, 760 kilotons TNT, 2.4 million tons TNT, etc). It just keeps going up!</p>\n<figure style=\"width: 320px\" class=\"wp-caption alignright\"><img src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-3Sx4piTmfP8/VHwIW_3hJ8I/AAAAAAAAA6M/kXrmgplT6r0/s1600/shuttle_atlantis.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"240\" border=\"0\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">The gorgeous space shuttle Atlantis</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Now let&#8217;s replace that 1-gram cube of sugar with the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle\">2,030-ton space shuttle</a>. That means we need to scale up the energy requirement by a factor of 1.84 billion (hahahaha, just nonsense numbers here). This means that at 99% light speed, the kinetic energy of the shuttle would be about the same as about 19.4 billion <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TNT_equivalent#Examples\">tons of TNT</a>. That&#8217;s more than twice the energy of the largest earthquakes ever recorded.</p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<p>Now just for kicks, let&#8217;s say we&#8217;re able to bypass limitations of relativity and just accelerate a dude smoothly up to light speed. Perhaps we have a source of infinite energy. Who knows? Anyway, we accelerate a dude without a spacecraft up to light speed. What&#8217;s the limitations there? Well, keep in mind that what most of us consider to be Earth&#8217;s gravity is an acceleration that our body feels as its pulled toward the Earth&#8217;s surface. You experience something like gravity whenever you accelerate in any direction, like the feeling of going up in an elevator (before the speed evens out), or that first bit of liftoff when you&#8217;re in an airplane.</p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-_Zo4IKymGg8/VHwJQTl8eHI/AAAAAAAAA6U/VJMmf1U3YHs/s1600/astronaut_gforce.png\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-_Zo4IKymGg8/VHwJQTl8eHI/AAAAAAAAA6U/VJMmf1U3YHs/s1600/astronaut_gforce.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"295\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>It&#8217;s turns out that\u00a0<a href=\"http://zidbits.com/2012/02/could-humans-colonize-a-planet-with-stronger-gravity/\">the human body has limitations\u00a0</a>on how much of that acceleration it can take, and that acceleration is measured in multiples of Earth&#8217;s gravity:\u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-force\">g-force</a>. We regularly experience 1g. Duh. The space shuttle would have pilots feeling 3g at maximum during liftoff and re-entry. \u00a0Remember, they undergo\u00a0<i>intense</i>\u00a0training to be able to handle that. If you undergo sustained acceleration at ~10g for more than a few minutes, you&#8217;re not walking away without serious damage. Hell, you&#8217;re <i>not</i> walking away. Let&#8217;s say for the sake of argument (again, ignoring relativity and practicality) that you accelerate constantly at 10g up to the speed of light. That would mean you&#8217;d have to be at a soul-destroying 10g\u00a0<u>constantly</u>\u00a0for 51,000 minutes, or a little over 35 days. You could, of course, just accelerate at 3g (which people can take, with difficulty) for 118 days, or 1g for a year, but where&#8217;s the fun in that?</p>\n<p>To sum up *some of* the limits of light speed:</p>\n<ol>\n<li>Humans can&#8217;t do it</li>\n<li>Accelerating near/up to it in reasonable timescales is really really uncomfortable</li>\n<li>Moving really fast requires truly insane amounts of energy</li>\n</ol>\n<p>This is just the first part of the question! More to come later this week!</p>\n<p><i>Keep in mind that these calculations are done on the fly and are often rounded. Check the links for more detail and keep in mind that I cut out relativity a lot because it complicates the hell out of things.</i></p>\n</div>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-19T02:32:35", "date_gmt": "2014-11-30T02:28:55", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=222", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=222", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/222"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/222/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/223", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=222", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=222"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>This academic quarter, I did something a little different than I normally do. In addition to simply assigning and grading labs as was required, I made an assignment of my own that my students were responsible for each week. They had the option of: \u2022 Finding an article about astronomy and writing a page about [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [13, 79, 80, 15, 81, 17, 82], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=222"}, "id": 222, "title": {"rendered": "Astro Questions: Difficulties with Light Speed"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-19T02:32:35", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "astro-questions-difficulties-with-light-speed", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 220, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-bright-truths-about-black-holes/", "date": "2014-11-11T02:25:02", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-KTwwMmyh1is/VGJJ3z5pibI/AAAAAAAAA4M/mbYNJM2LNt8/s1600/blackhole2.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-KTwwMmyh1is/VGJJ3z5pibI/AAAAAAAAA4M/mbYNJM2LNt8/s1600/blackhole2.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"208\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>Black holes are one of those mysteries of science that sci-fi writers like to toy with. They&#8217;re menacing! They huge! They have some mysterious cosmic sucking power! I want to clear the air here about these magnificent, mysterious objects. Note, this isn&#8217;t going to be a comprehensive or exhaustive explanation about black holes. Just a little bit more information to clear a lot of the unknown.</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<p>Let&#8217;s start with their basic nature. We talk about black holes in terms of mass because, technically, the actual objects actually have no size. Let&#8217;s separate two things: the <b>singularity</b>\u00a0is the actual collapsed object that has all the mass; the <b>event horizon </b>(the &#8220;black hole&#8221;) encompasses the region of space where light cannot escape. More on the latter in a bit.</p>\n<div class=\"p1\">\n<p>What do I mean by a &#8220;collapsed&#8221; object? To get that, we need to go back to <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitation\">gravity</a>\u00a0(which is, after all, <i><a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution\">only\u00a0a theory</a></i>). The force of gravity is the attraction felt by any two objects that have mass (what we think of as weight). The bigger the mass of either object, the stronger the force of gravity. The larger the distance between them, the weaker the force. One way to think of it is like this: mass can be thought of as an amount that something likes to pull and be pulled. The more mass it has, the more it wants to pull and be pulled. However, the further away it is, the harder it is to actually pull. <b>Every</b> object is gravitationally attracted to <b>every</b> other object <b>everywhere</b> at <b>all times</b>. Me to my laptop (<a href=\"http://www.geekwithlaptop.com/wp-content/uploads/2010/03/technology-addiction.jpg\">more of an addiction</a> than an attraction), my phone to the floor, the Moon to my shoe, the Andromeda galaxy to the left wheel of your car\u2014as long as two objects have mass, they will be attracted to one another.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"p1\">\n<p><a href=\"http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-cOpMC72RSaE/VGJOEfTRH_I/AAAAAAAAA4o/lHY3hNqW-JU/s1600/singularity.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-cOpMC72RSaE/VGJOEfTRH_I/AAAAAAAAA4o/lHY3hNqW-JU/s1600/singularity.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"229\" border=\"0\" /></a>In a star, all the mass in the star is feeling the force of gravity of itself, and is constantly trying to pull itself together. This is countered by the force from energy generated by nuclear fusion (<a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-drop-that-stellar-beat-the-kappa-mechanism/\">discussed a bit here</a>). These two processes together act a lot <a href=\"http://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Main/TheWallsAreClosingIn\">like a hero trying to stop the walls from closing in on her</a>. If the star&#8217;s core stops producing energy (the hero&#8217;s strength fails), the star&#8217;s entire mass collapses toward the center (the walls crush her to death). This is what happens <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova#Core_collapse\">at the end of the life of a high-mass star</a>. If the star is massive enough, the attractive force of gravity of its collapsing core is stronger than any repulsion that could possibly stop it, and all that matter gets crushed together. Down from the size of a star to smaller than a planet. Smaller still\u2014smaller than an apple. Smaller than a penny! Smaller even than that little piece of dust floating through the air. Smaller than the smallest thing you can possibly imagine! In fact, it becomes so small that to give it a numerical physical size doesn&#8217;t even make sense anymore\u2014we describe it as being a singularity that&#8217;s &#8220;infinitesimal&#8221;. I think of it as a fast way of saying &#8220;infinitely small&#8221;.</p>\n</div>\n<div class=\"p1\">\n<p>Ok so the &#8220;black hole&#8221; part itself. Remember that the collapsed singularity retains all that mass from before. With objects that aren&#8217;t black holes but do have mass, there&#8217;s a certain speed (velocity) that you can travel to leave their gravitational pull (escape). It&#8217;s called the <b>escape velocity</b>\u00a0(we&#8217;re not creative people), and is a minimum speed limit controlled by the mass of the object and your distance from it.\u00a0As an example, I have about 80 kg of mass. And for simplicity&#8217;s sake, let&#8217;s crush me down to a sphere of radius 50 centimeters. If you&#8217;re standing right next to me, then to escape from <b>my</b> gravitational pull on <b>you</b> (note, your mass doesn&#8217;t matter), you&#8217;ve got to move at 0.00045 miles per hour. So, you know, <a href=\"https://s-media-cache-ec0.pinimg.com/originals/61/02/de/6102debe7cf78ec19ad1f86dc17d0092.jpg\">you could just walk away</a>. To escape the Earth&#8217;s surface, you have to move at 25,000 mph (which is why we need rockets). Now, the fastest any object can move is the speed of light, 670 million mph. So, with black holes, we define the hole&#8217;s size (or event horizon) as the distance from that central mass out to where the escape velocity is equal to the speed of light. Within that distance, the escape velocity is higher than light speed, so <b>within the event horizon nothing can possibly escape</b>\u00a0(<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hawking_radiation\">except for some extremely special circumstances</a>). Since not even light can escape from the event horizon, that region of space within the event horizon is black. It emits no light, and zero light is black. Again, astronomers are not creative people.</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-OEXHtlUoWLo/VGJM4KOZeyI/AAAAAAAAA4Y/TMH9HDo9JOQ/s1600/black-hole-milky-way.jpg\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-OEXHtlUoWLo/VGJM4KOZeyI/AAAAAAAAA4Y/TMH9HDo9JOQ/s1600/black-hole-milky-way.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /></a>So with that, let&#8217;s dispel our first myth: the sizes of black holes. Even for supremely-massive singularities, the black holes are fairly small. A black hole with <b>the mass of the our Sun</b> would only have an event horizon <b>less than 3.7 miles in diameter. </b><a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sagittarius_A*\">The supermassive black hole at the center of our galaxy</a>\u00a0has a mass equal to about 4.5 million times our own Sun&#8217;s mass. It&#8217;s event horizon? About<i>at the center of our galaxy</i>\u00a0would fit within the\u00a0<i>smallest orbit in our Solar System</i>. Black holes are freakin&#8217; tiny.</p>\n<p>0.3 astronomical units (AU) in diameter. For reference, the planet Mercury orbits the Sun from about 0.3 AU away. So, the black hole part of the black hole</p>\n<p>Now, we&#8217;ve already established that once something enters within the event horizon, it&#8217;s (basically) gone forever. Many people get concerned that black holes suck up\u00a0everything they can\u00a0(<a href=\"http://www.physics.umaine.edu/ncomins/blackh_landingpage.html\">black holes don&#8217;t suck</a>), grow bigger, and eat even more. They imagine this exponentially growing beast that cannot be stopped! Let&#8217;s kill that right now. If you replaced the Sun with a black hole of the same mass, <b>nothing would change</b>. The planet&#8217;s orbit depends on three things: the mass of the planet, the mass of the thing they&#8217;re orbiting, and the distance between the two. <i>Not a single one of these things would change</i>. So, if the Sun&#8217;s not actively gobbling matter then neither would a black hole with the Sun&#8217;s mass. Even if it <i>were</i>\u00a0gobbling up matter, if it were to consume <b><u>all</u></b> of the matter within the Solar System (planets, moons, asteroids, and comets; 0.0001% of the Sun&#8217;s mass), its event horizon would only increase by 6 millimeters in diameter. Astronomically, that&#8217;s basically nothing.</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-YKAMfZMNrEs/VGJNuSYAXEI/AAAAAAAAA4g/eNyYIlzhvYw/s1600/gobble.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-YKAMfZMNrEs/VGJNuSYAXEI/AAAAAAAAA4g/eNyYIlzhvYw/s1600/gobble.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"200\" height=\"190\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>What about that demon at the Galaxy&#8217;s center? Well, it didn&#8217;t get to 4.5 million times the Sun&#8217;s mass for no reason\u2014it&#8217;s been quite busy gobbling stuff nearby. Still, it&#8217;s not just vacuuming up everything in the Galaxy. For that black hole to have the same pull on our Sun as the Sun has on the Earth, it&#8217;d have to be about 19.5 light years. Not only is the closest black hole discovered thus far <a href=\"http://blackholes.stardate.org/objects/type.php?p=stellar-mass\">about 3,000 light years away</a>, but we are over 24,000 light years from that supermassive black hole in our Galaxy&#8217;s center. Well beyond its influence.</p>\n<p>What IF a black hole was nearby? In fact, what if the <a href=\"http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2012/10/19/large-hadron-collider-court_n_1989630.html\">insane worries of certain folk</a> were true and our recently-minted Large Hadron Collider produced its own black holes? Well, the LHC smashes two proton beams together in bunches at high fractions of the speed of light (&gt; 99%). They come in 2,808 bunches, with 115 billion protons per bunch, for a total of about 323 trillion protons. If something were to mash all these protons together into a singularity, their combined mass wouldn&#8217;t even equal 1% of one grain of salt. Its event horizon would be imperceptibly small (less than one trillionth of a trillionth of the radius of</p>\n<p>an electron), and if theory holds, it&#8217;d evaporate almost as soon as it was created. Even factoring the energy boost by moving near the speed of light (in general relativity, that increases the mass of an object by a ton), nothing changes.</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-RyQiffAgAtU/VGJO67EM1SI/AAAAAAAAA4w/67l5jhkuWfw/s1600/lhc_collision.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-RyQiffAgAtU/VGJO67EM1SI/AAAAAAAAA4w/67l5jhkuWfw/s1600/lhc_collision.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"400\" height=\"225\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p><a href=\"http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-rNYj5HBmhHY/VGJPX3Pb-9I/AAAAAAAAA48/ADTZdMA4Xds/s1600/blackholes_accretion.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-rNYj5HBmhHY/VGJPX3Pb-9I/AAAAAAAAA48/ADTZdMA4Xds/s1600/blackholes_accretion.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"266\" border=\"0\" /></a>Lastly, I want to talk about observing black holes. Just because they don&#8217;t emit any light of their own doesn&#8217;t mean that they&#8217;re undetectable either by light or any other means. Swirling around many black holes, especially those in densely-packed areas, is an extremely hot disk of material called the <b><a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accretion_disc\">accretion disc</a></b>. &#8220;Accretion&#8221; is really just a fancier way of saying &#8220;collection&#8221;, so an accretion disk is just the disk of material collected by a black hole&#8217;s gravity (we&#8217;re gonna completely ignore any complications from magnetic fields). The material travels around the black hole looking like a frisbee with a hole in the center. Toward the very edge of the event horizon, the material is traveling extremely fast, but that speed drops off dramatically as you get toward the outer edge of the disk. It helps to think of the disk as a series of rings all touching each other and moving at different speeds. Because you have these rings of material with different speeds rubbing against each other, you get friction. And as you know if you&#8217;ve ever rubbed your hands against each other in the winter, a little bit of friction can generate a decent amount of heat. A lot of friction in the interior of the disk generates a ton of heat\u2014so much that the accretion disk not only glows in visible light and UV, but practically <i>screams</i>\u00a0in X-rays! So, when we look in the galaxy with our X-ray telescopes, we spot black holes (really black hole accretion disks) left and right.</p>\n<p>Goodness, I want to write more, I really do. But this has gone on pretty long already, so I&#8217;m going to wrap it up here. I&#8217;ll end this little discussion with this: the physical Universe is an intricate, at times subtle construct. It&#8217;s a place where both little things have huge consequences and massive things don&#8217;t change much at all. If you think that what you see on the TV or movie screen is amazing, just wait until you learn about what <i>actually</i>\u00a0happens at all levels of existence!</p>\n<p><i>Disclaimer: As is per usual, this is MOST CERTAINLY NOT to be taken as a definitive source of information. I&#8217;ve &#8220;borrowed&#8221; <u>heavily</u>\u00a0from Wikipedia. I&#8217;ve also heavily abused the laws of physics and glossed over a bunch of details about magnetic fields, spinning black holes, and other things. The vast majority here applies though. Feel free to ask questions and demand clarity. Always always always ask questions and demand clarity.</i></p>\n</div>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-19T02:28:02", "date_gmt": "2014-11-11T02:25:02", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=219", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=219", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/219"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/219/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/220", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=219", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=219"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>Black holes are one of those mysteries of science that sci-fi writers like to toy with. They&#8217;re menacing! They huge! They have some mysterious cosmic sucking power! I want to clear the air here about these magnificent, mysterious objects. Note, this isn&#8217;t going to be a comprehensive or exhaustive explanation about black holes. Just a [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [77, 78, 16, 23, 24], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=219"}, "id": 219, "title": {"rendered": "Science Sunday: Bright Truths about Black Holes"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-19T02:28:02", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "science-sunday-bright-truths-about-black-holes", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 217, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-drop-that-stellar-beat-the-kappa-mechanism/", "date": "2014-09-13T02:20:56", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-JkZKv9ym05M/VBTi7bEV1YI/AAAAAAAAA1k/KtUjPzVzoio/s1600/soundwave6.png\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-JkZKv9ym05M/VBTi7bEV1YI/AAAAAAAAA1k/KtUjPzVzoio/s1600/soundwave6.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"213\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>One of the most fascinating things to me in our Universe is the fact that stars, these ridiculously massive, hot, bright objects that provide the necessary energy for existence as we know it, are not at all static objects. We see our own Sun in the sky, and we really\u00a0only\u00a0see one small bright yellow disc. Look a little closer (never look directly at the Sun!), and you see its surface changing every 11 years. Sunspots grow and disappear, prominences arc wildly over its surface, flares spew out light, energy, and a cornucopia of charged particles into space, etc. And ours is just a run-of-the-mill Main Sequence star! What I want to talk about here is something that exhibits the dynamic side of evolved versions of stars like ours: stellar pulsation.</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<p>Now, while stars upon close examination are indeed dynamic objects, they do have a tendency to at least maintain their size. <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-the-stars-part-i-introduction-to-shiny-things/\">As has been discussed before</a>, all stars produce nuclear energy in their cores. This energy heats up the gas in the star, and provides the necessary gas pressure needed to hold the star up against the collapse of all its considerable mass. The more massive the star, the more energy needed to hold it up against the collapse of its fatness.\u00a0\u00a0If the star were to contract a small bit, the increased gravitational pressure would cause the core to produce more energy, thereby increasing the gas pressure, restoring it to its equilibrium state. This maintenance of shape by the interplay between gravitational collapse and gas pressure is called <b>hydrostatic equilibrium</b>. Be thankful for it. We&#8217;d never have had a chance to exist without it!</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-2pQp2nWLz4o/VBTj71WA7eI/AAAAAAAAA1s/tbtv7FgXrNs/s1600/pulsation.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-2pQp2nWLz4o/VBTj71WA7eI/AAAAAAAAA1s/tbtv7FgXrNs/s1600/pulsation.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"400\" height=\"300\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>So, what does hydrostatic equilibrium have to do with stellar pulsation (and in particular, the <b><a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kappa_mechanism\">kappa mechanism</a></b>)? Well, all types of stellar pulsations occur when this equilibrium state is thrown out of whack. For massive thermal pulses, like those that occur in <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch\">the stars that I study for my thesis</a>, the star&#8217;s balanced state is disrupted by periodic changes in the type of fusion occurring in its core. However, I&#8217;ll save that little discussion for another post (if you really want the technical nitty gritty though, check out <a href=\"http://articles.adsabs.harvard.edu/full/1983ARA%26A..21..271I\">this 1983 review paper by Icko Iben and Alvio Renzini</a> on AGB star evolution). For the shorter-term pulsations that I want to focus on here, the balance is ruined by changes in the type and properties of gas in the star.</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-5pVUo7ZOKYc/VBTkKXlmpzI/AAAAAAAAA10/kwCtaRu5A9Q/s1600/opacity.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-5pVUo7ZOKYc/VBTkKXlmpzI/AAAAAAAAA10/kwCtaRu5A9Q/s1600/opacity.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"188\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>I want to take a brief little detour here and talk a little bit about <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opacity_(optics)\">opacity</a>. Opacity, denoted by the greek letter &#8220;kappa&#8221;, tells you how difficult it is for light to penetrate a substance. Something with a low opacity will let light pass through easily (like a window, dry air, or the vacuum of space). Opaque substances block most or all light from passing through (like a lamp shade, or a wall). When an opaque thing interacts with light, it&#8217;ll either scatter that light away, or absorb it.</p>\n<p>Right, so that&#8217;s opacity. In stars, the opacity of the gas in the star typically decreases as temperature and density of the gas increases. This means that as you move inward from the surface toward the stellar core, the gas becomes less and less opaque to light (as temperature and density increase as you get close to the core). \u00a0This also means that if the star contracts a little bit (increasing the temperature and density of the interior), the opacity of its atmosphere decreases and allows radiation to escape more easily, reaching the gas in that star&#8217;s atmosphere and restoring that equilibrium state. However, in stars that are unstable to opacity-driven pulsations, there exists a layer of gas (typically partially-ionized hydrogen or helium) in the stellar atmosphere whose opacity <i>increases</i>\u00a0as temperature and density increase. Thus, if the star were to contract a little, that layer would become <i>more</i>\u00a0opaque, and radiation would have an increasingly difficult time permeating the atmosphere.</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-57KyX0etniw/VBTkmeCDSDI/AAAAAAAAA18/ZyGGlh4qfGI/s1600/piston_engine.gif\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-57KyX0etniw/VBTkmeCDSDI/AAAAAAAAA18/ZyGGlh4qfGI/s1600/piston_engine.gif\" alt=\"\" border=\"0\" /></a>Now here&#8217;s the kicker. If the star contracts, the gas in that opaque layer heats up. \u00a0When the gas heats up, it becomes ionized and that ionized form is more opaque than the non-ionized form. \u00a0The radiation is going into\u00a0fully <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionization\">ionizing the gas</a> in that layer and heating up the gas below it, though not creating gas pressure IN that opaque layer to counteract gravity. So, the star continues to shrink until the buildup of radiative pressure below that opaque layer is just too much and the gas has reached maximum opacity. Then, just like a piston with heated gas beneath it, the cumulative radiative pressure forces the star&#8217;s atmosphere outward!</p>\n<p>The gas beneath that piston then cools, allowing the it to come back down to its rest state. Same thing happens in a star\u2014the buildup of radiative pressure gets released, the gas becomes cooler, less ionized, and less opaque, and so the star can contract again. \u00a0And thus we have the lub-dub of evolved stars. Radiative pressure builds beneath the opaque layer until it&#8217;s just too much and expands outward. The cooled gas (and thus lower gas pressure) allows the star&#8217;s atmosphere to contract and build its opacity back up. Then pump back out, and &#8220;relax&#8221; in.</p>\n<figure style=\"width: 231px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-OMDM0Jqg2-4/VBTljvivxJI/AAAAAAAAA2Q/k2afNLFmzmA/s1600/cepheid.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"231\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Credit: <a href=\"http://www.phys.ufl.edu/~buchler/ceph/\">http://www.phys.ufl.edu/~buchler/ceph/</a></figcaption></figure>\n<p>And I think that is damn cool.For some stars (Cepheid variables), this pulsation cycle can range between 1 to 100 days. For other stars (RR Lyrae), this pulsation can happen in under a day. These pulsations can change a star&#8217;s radius \u00a0by some ridiculous values (25% for some Cepheids!), and their brightnesses can just about double (RR Lyrae and Cepheids)! We know that the duration of the pulsation is linked to the average brightness of the star itself (called the period-luminosity relationship for variable stars. Thank <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henrietta_Swan_Leavitt\">Henrietta Swan Leavitt</a> for that one). As such, we can use stars that show these variations to calculate distances and map things out in 3D space! Just from that beat, that little stellar instability, we can learn about the layout of our local Universe.</p>\n<p>-Peace</p>\n<p>References:</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://www.wikipedia.org\">http://www.wikipedia.org<br />\n</a>The magic of Google<br />\n<a href=\"http://www.astronomynotes.com/starsun/s7.htm\">http://www.astronomynotes.com/starsun/s7.htm<br />\n</a><a href=\"http://www.space.com/15396-variable-stars.html\">http://www.space.com/15396-variable-stars.html<br />\n</a><a href=\"http://www.astro.princeton.edu/~gk/A403/pulse.pdf\">http://www.astro.princeton.edu/~gk/A403/pulse.pdf</a></p>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-19T02:24:32", "date_gmt": "2014-09-13T02:20:56", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=216", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=216", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/216"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/216/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/217", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=216", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=216"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>One of the most fascinating things to me in our Universe is the fact that stars, these ridiculously massive, hot, bright objects that provide the necessary energy for existence as we know it, are not at all static objects. Most of the music that I listen to is in that genre, and is pretty well restricted to the 1996-2003 era. During that time, the lyrical content of hip hop songs came under heavy fire. Parents and governmental agencies alike attacked the music and the culture for promoting violence, disrespect for authority, and particularly, profane language. \u00a0I remember those debates well, and I vividly remember not being allowed to listen to songs or certain radio stations because of their lyrical content.</p>\n<p>Now I&#8217;m somewhat older, questionably wiser, far more of an analyst, and am possessed with a similar level of curiosity for the world as I had when I heard my first Biggie track. I&#8217;ve played my favorite songs over and over again, chock full of the profanity that I missed as a kid, and I noticed that yeah, those songs are really, really not for kids. \u00a0But I could only feel that way one track at a time. \u00a0I get my 3 minutes and 30 seconds to make my judgement, then it&#8217;s on to the next (on-on to the next) one. \u00a0Quantitatively, however, I don&#8217;t really have a clue just how thick it is. \u00a0As such, I&#8217;ve decided to join two of my loves\u2014data analysis and hip hop culture\u2014and answer the following question: just how much vulgarity exists in the heights of hip hop?<!--more--></p>\n<figure style=\"width: 400px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-ibx95nd5-8E/U5QC2HSm2BI/AAAAAAAAArw/Vw-HDj41VC4/s1600/albummosaic.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"400\" height=\"300\" border=\"0\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">I got 99 albums&#8230;literally. This actually worked out to 99 albums!</figcaption></figure>\n<p><b>The Source</b></p>\n<div>\n<p>I didn&#8217;t initially know where to get a comprehensive list of top hip hop albums, so I looked at <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_best-selling_hip_hop_albums_in_the_United_States\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">Wikipedia</a>. There&#8217;s this nice little table of &#8220;<span style=\"color: #252525; font-family: sans-serif;\">best-selling\u00a0<a title=\"Hip hop music\" href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hip_hop_music\" data-blogger-escaped-style=\"background-attachment: initial; background-clip: initial; background-color: initial; background-image: none; background-origin: initial; background-position: initial; background-repeat: initial; background-size: initial; color: #0b0080; text-decoration: none;\">hip hop</a>\u00a0albums in the\u00a0<a title=\"United States\" href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States\" data-blogger-escaped-style=\"background-attachment: initial; background-clip: initial; background-color: initial; background-image: none; background-origin: initial; background-position: initial; background-repeat: initial; background-size: initial; color: #0b0080; text-decoration: none;\">United States</a></span><span style=\"color: #252525; font-family: sans-serif;\">\u00a0according to the\u00a0</span><a title=\"Recording Industry Association of America\" href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America\" data-blogger-escaped-style=\"background: none rgb(255, 255, 255); color: #0b0080; font-family: sans-serif; font-size: 14px; line-height: 22.399999618530273px; text-decoration: none;\">Recording Industry Association of America</a>.&#8221; As far I could tell, it was last updated in 2013. No matter, I could still rock with it.</p>\n<p>I got as many song lyrics as I could for those albums from <a href=\"http://www.songlyrics.com/\">songlyrics.com</a>. \u00a0It was nice because they repeat the chorus and keep a lot of the ad libs, so I&#8217;m getting the most that I can.\u00a0Unfortunately, I ended up missing 8 albums because they were either not found or weren&#8217;t categorized right. \u00a0Even so, I still managed to look at 99 albums, for a total of 1,472 tracks! Sure, these don&#8217;t represent <b><i>all</i></b> of hip hop (can&#8217;t really rep <i>all </i>of anything), but these are the best selling albums of all time. As such, <b>these albums are the way that hip hop and we that listen to it present ourselves to the world.</b>\u00a0Something to keep in mind moving forward.</p>\n<p><b>The Result</b></p>\n<p>I looked at 3 of your run-of-the-mill offensive words, and of course the notorious N-word. The results&#8230;well, see for yourself.</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-zaPHy3ZtcrA/U5QFp-_7BiI/AAAAAAAAAr8/6ubg2j9D4tg/s1600/money_plot.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-zaPHy3ZtcrA/U5QFp-_7BiI/AAAAAAAAAr8/6ubg2j9D4tg/s1600/money_plot.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"560\" height=\"640\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>What you should see above these words are two graphs of word usage over time. \u00a0The first graph is counts per year for each year since 1985. What this means is I totaled all the instances that particular word was used in every track in every album (that was available to me) that came out that year. For the N-word I included every possible variation I could manage. I wasn&#8217;t as diligent with the others (I added b&#8211;ch and b&#8211;ches together, but didn&#8217;t give enough damns to find more &#8220;f&#8211;k&#8221;s), but their close cousins don&#8217;t add much to their totals. The bottom graph is similar to the top one, but accounts for differences in album sales. Think of it as the word usage per album on average.</p>\n<p>So&#8230;wow. \u00a0I&#8217;d love to say something about the explosion of vulgarity in 1993 and how it&#8217;s been sustained over time, but that blue line is startling. What does this say? Best-selling rappers love saying the N-word. Obvious. Sure, the other ones have pretty bad frequencies too (does anyone really <i>need</i>\u00a0to say &#8220;b&#8211;ch&#8221; 40 times per album?), but nothing compares to the N-word. It&#8217;s no wonder why we&#8217;re often seen as that which the word describes\u2014we say it ALL THE DAMN TIME! Hell, it could be said that in order to make the top albums list, you need to pepper in as many n&#8211;gas as you can while still maintaining some degree of grammar (I&#8217;m looking at you, YG). Thankfully it&#8217;s decreased over time, though I&#8217;m skeptical. Still, we as carriers of this culture just need to do better.</p>\n<p><b>Finally&#8230;</b></p>\n<p>The <a href=\"http://www.cmonsontv.com/\">C&#8217;mon Son</a> award goes to DMX. As a career scientist, it&#8217;s a habit of mine to qualify every statement that I make with a conditional; something to cover my ass if I turn out to be wrong. However, here I espouse my beliefs, and it is my sincere belief that everything about the world that is known can be known by anyone willing to put in the effort. I experience this on a regular basis with my forays into the Universe as an astronomer, and I&#8217;ve revisited this when I&#8217;ve set about learning new languages\u2014spoken or programming\u2014and new skills (physical or otherwise).</p>\n<div>\n<p>The fact of the matter is this: whether it&#8217;s language, or math, or finance, or dance, or microbiology, all spheres of human knowledge were discovered and fleshed out by human minds working with the raw material of the human brain. \u00a0And barring some developmental disorders, most brains at base have the same machinery. That means, at least to me, that if a human mind worked out the details of quantum electrodynamics, mine can as well. If a dance instructor created some choreography for me to learn, I can work at it and make that choreography my own. I can make art as great as the greats. I can build, I can learn, I can do. What it takes is the effort on my part to achieve.</p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<p>I feel like this is something that we forget as we venture further into adulthood, settling deeper into our own comfort zones. We get accustomed to being bad at things, and become unwilling to rectify that badness. For example, when faced with a complicated math problem,\u00a0many people will wear their inability to do math as a badge of honor. As a TA of 3 years I&#8217;ve encountered many versions of this. However, in reality it amounts to the same thing: you aren&#8217;t bad at math, you just haven&#8217;t made the effort to understand it. I make the same sort of statements with doing social things. &#8220;I&#8217;m not very sociable&#8221;, or &#8220;I&#8217;m bad at small talk&#8221;, or &#8220;I&#8217;ve never understood that way of existence.&#8221;</p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<p>You may have had bad teachers of certain subjects, or bad experiences with certain realms of life, but the ability to understand and BE something that you&#8217;ve never been before lies within you. It&#8217;s the only way you&#8217;ve become the person you are today. You weren&#8217;t, as an infant, born into the world with the knowledge of how to turn an acid and a base into salt and water. \u00a0Humanity didn&#8217;t come pre-installed with knowledge of agriculture, pottery, architecture, electrical engineering, or relationship psychology. These are all things that have been learned and\u00a0developed by individuals with a thirst for growth; growth of themselves and of humanity.</p>\n</div>\n<div>\n<p>So don&#8217;t let your education end with your degree, or your career, or your thoughts about yourself. \u00a0Access your curiosity. Consume your ignorance and grow. Learn about life until you&#8217;re being lowered into your grave. Become that which you&#8217;ve nor anyone else has\u00a0<a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-GpkBShEE2QM/U3QmcEU64NI/AAAAAAAAAp4/Mi_TN7_EUeo/s1600/Ouroboros.png\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-GpkBShEE2QM/U3QmcEU64NI/AAAAAAAAAp4/Mi_TN7_EUeo/s1600/Ouroboros.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"200\" height=\"150\" border=\"0\" /></a>ever seen before.</p>\n</div>\n<div></div>\n<div>\n<p>&#8212;End</p>\n</div>\n<div></div>\n<div>\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n</div>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-19T02:17:14", "date_gmt": "2014-05-14T02:15:05", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=209", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=209", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/209"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/209/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/210", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=209", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=209"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>&#8220;Nothing is too wonderful to be true, if it be consistent with the laws of Nature&#8221; &#8211; Michael Faraday, father of Magnetism The topic of learning and changing oneself through effort and education has been coming up a lot for me lately. \u00a0As such, I decided that since I hadn&#8217;t written a blog post since [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [31, 26, 2, 28, 74], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=209"}, "id": 209, "title": {"rendered": "An Eternity of Learning"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-19T02:17:14", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "an-eternity-of-learning", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 207, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/repost-black-americans-and-afro-latinos-a-common-history/", "date": "2014-02-14T02:13:02", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-b2i_T7lCdQc/Uv5JnPhpyXI/AAAAAAAAAn8/3_U97A6tMdU/s1600/Afro-Brazilians-Now-Brazils-Majority.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-b2i_T7lCdQc/Uv5JnPhpyXI/AAAAAAAAAn8/3_U97A6tMdU/s1600/Afro-Brazilians-Now-Brazils-Majority.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"256\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" /></a></p>\n<p>Not much to put forth for an introduction here. \u00a0This is an article examining the connection between black Americans and Afro-Latinos. \u00a0A good read, especially for Black History Month. Interesting point raised in the article: Brazil has the highest black population in the world today outside of Africa, with the United States coming in third. \u00a0Check out more here:</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://www.africanglobe.net/headlines/black-americans-afro-latinos-common-history/\">http://www.africanglobe.net/headlines/black-americans-afro-latinos-common-history/</a></p>\n<p>A related article about Afro-Brazilians being the majority population in Brazil here:</p>\n<p><a
