You rut like pigs, is all. You got the most in you, and you use the least. You hear me, you? Got a million in you and spend pennies. Got a genius in you and think crazies. Got a heart in you and feel empties. All a you. Every you\u2026 \u2026Take a war to make you spend. Take a jam to make you think. Take a challenge to make you great. Rest of the time you sit around lazy, you. Pigs, you! All right, God damn you! I challenge you, me. Die or live and be great. Blow yourselves to Christ gone or come and find me, Gully Foyle, and I make you men. I make you great. I give you the stars. &#8211; <strong>Gulliver Foyle</strong>, <em>The Stars my Destination</em></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Mediocrity is an issue for me. Mediocrity, <a href=\"http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/mediocre\">by one definition</a>, means &#8220;of only ordinary or moderate quality; neither good nor bad; barely adequate.&#8221; By another definition, it means &#8220;not satisfactory; poor; inferior.&#8221; When mediocrity comes to mind, the latter meaning is what I envision. The thing about mediocrity is that once it&#8217;s accepted, it can go on to generate a trend in your life. Paraphrasing (as I don&#8217;t remember the exact verbage, only the gist of it) what my AP History teacher Mr. Burke had said to us roughly 10 years ago: you can be mediocre now, and go on to a mediocre school, get a mediocre job, a mediocre car, a mediocre house, a mediocre wife, and a mediocre life. And that&#8217;s fine, if that&#8217;s what you&#8217;re comfortable with.</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<div align=\"LEFT\">\n<p><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">The reason why mediocrity is an issue for me, though, is that it&#8217;s REALLY BORING! Yes, you can live life sitting solidly in mediocrity, but what the hell is the point? What does it serve you to \u201cjust get by\u201d all the time? I&#8217;d rather live extraordinarily. In fact, that&#8217;s just about all that I respect and revere. Life is short, so why am I going to waste it by doing and witnessing the average? I want to see and do the spectacular, the passionate, the dynamic, and the strange.</span></p>\n</div>\n<div align=\"LEFT\">\n<p><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">That&#8217;s part of why I study what I study: the average for the Universe is the extraordinary when compared to what happens on Earth. That&#8217;s not even the whole of it though, because the physics that I study also applies to what we experience on Earth and makes that extraordinary. I guess what I really want is to see the extraordinary in existence. That seems truer to my desire. Physics and astronomy have given me the tools and the practice necessary to train my mind&#8217;s eye to see the world as more than just what it first appears to be. Yellow paint isn&#8217;t just yellow paint to me; yellow paint is a liquid composition of various atoms and molecules whose bonds are configured in such a way where that liquid absorbs the majority of all other visible radiation, but reflects wavelengths corresponding to the color yellow into my eye. That liquid itself is composed of billions of billions of billions (this is a low-ball estimate by the way) of atoms and molecules, all vibrating, rotating, stretching, and compressing, bouncing off of one another in a beautifully random dance. And each atom within those molecules is itself a universe of the extraordinary, with the individual physics governing each electron and each atomic nucleus. \u00a0The nucleus itself is further another realm of spectacular events, and all the way downward in scale. \u00a0All within yellow paint.</span></p>\n</div>\n<div align=\"LEFT\">\n<p><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">The ordinary&#8211;the mediocre&#8211;simply moves life forward to the next step. \u00a0The extraordinary&#8211;being the extraordinary, seeing the extraordinary, living the extraordinary&#8211;makes life worth living.</span></p>\n</div>\n<div align=\"LEFT\">\n<p><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">With that said, I leave you with a couple of quotes from books by the author of the quote at the top of the page, Alfred Bester. \u00a0He&#8217;s written two of my favorite books, and has a few things to say about greatness.</span></p>\n</div>\n<div align=\"LEFT\">\n<blockquote><p>\u201cIf a man&#8217;s got talent and guts to buck society, he&#8217;s obviously above average. You want to hold on to him. You straighten him out and turn him into a plus value. Why throw him away? Do that enough and all you&#8217;ve got left are the sheep.\u201d</p></blockquote>\n</div>\n<div align=\"LEFT\">\n<p><span style=\"font-family: Arial, Helvetica, sans-serif;\">&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;&#8212;-</span></p>\n</div>\n<div align=\"LEFT\">\n<blockquote><p>\u201cThere&#8217;s got to be more to life than just living,&#8221; Foyle said to the robot.</p>\n<p>&#8220;Then find it for yourself, sir. Don&#8217;t ask the world to stop moving because you have doubts.&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8220;Why can&#8217;t we all move forward together?&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8220;Because you&#8217;re all different. You&#8217;re not lemmings. Some must lead, and hope that the rest will follow.&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8220;Who leads?&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8220;The men who must&#8230;driven men, compelled men.&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8220;Freak men.&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8220;You&#8217;re all freaks, sir. But you always have been freaks. Life is a freak. That&#8217;s its hope and glory.\u201d</p></blockquote>\n</div>\n<div align=\"LEFT\">\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n</div>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-18T02:57:36", "date_gmt": "2012-07-24T02:47:50", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=100", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=100", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/100"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/100/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/101", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=100", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=100"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>You pigs, you. You rut like pigs, is all. You got the most in you, and you use the least. You hear me, you? Got a million in you and spend pennies. Got a genius in you and think crazies. Got a heart in you and feel empties. All a you. Every you\u2026 \u2026Take a [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [13, 31, 26, 29, 2, 28, 32, 37, 39], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=100"}, "id": 100, "title": {"rendered": "Thoughts on Mediocrity"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-18T02:57:36", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "thoughts-on-mediocrity", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 96, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-the-stars-part-ii-all-types-of-shiny-things/", "date": "2012-07-21T02:27:26", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/300px-Starsinthesky.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter wp-image-96 size-full\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/300px-Starsinthesky.jpg?resize=300%2C192\" alt=\"300px-Starsinthesky\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>Oh snap! It&#8217;s that time again!</p>\n<p>On the first Science Sunday, <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-the-stars-part-i-introduction-to-shiny-things/\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">I discussed the Sun</a>. \u00a0After that, I talked about <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-light-you-magnificent-bastard/\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">charged particles and light</a>. \u00a0The goal there was to talk about light and charged particles in order to build up the machinery for this and later blog posts, where I hope to talk about all of the wonderful things that they do in the Universe. \u00a0Very specifically, I wanted to introduce the concept of different types of light occurring at different wavelengths, so that I had a reference for when I talk about it here. \u00a0I&#8217;ll probably be referencing it often, so be sure to keep an eye out for links like this that use it as a reference.</p>\n<p>In great contrast to the first post, today I&#8217;m going to talk about the Sun not being special at all. \u00a0Instead I&#8217;ll cover most of the known types of stars that reside in our Milky Way, including the Sun as one amongst billions. \u00a0Today we talk about stellar populations.</p>\n<p>As with before, I&#8217;ll steal from Wikipedia liberally. \u00a0Onward!</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_97\" style=\"width: 480px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/480px-HRDiagram.png\"><img class=\"size-full wp-image-97\" src=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/480px-HRDiagram.png?resize=480%2C547\" alt=\"Hertzsprung-Russell Diagram/Color-Magnitude Diagram; This is one of many ways of visualizing it\" srcset=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/480px-HRDiagram.png?w=480 480w, http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/480px-HRDiagram.png?resize=263%2C300 263w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 480px) 100vw, 480px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Hertzsprung-Russell Diagram/Color-Magnitude Diagram; This is one of many ways of visualizing it</figcaption></figure>\n<p>So if we&#8217;re going to be talking about stellar populations in bulk, then it would probably be responsible of me to start with the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HR_Diagram\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">Hertzprung-Russell Diagram</a>. \u00a0This diagram is a convenient little ditty that was devised separately by scientists Ejnar Hertzsprung and Henry Norris Russell in order to see the relationships between the temperatures and luminosities (read: brightnesses) of stars. \u00a0It&#8217;s also known as a <b>color-magnitude diagram</b>, as color corresponds to the temperature of a given star, and magnitude corresponds to the inherent brightness of that star. \u00a0It&#8217;s pretty clearly shown above.</p>\n<p>Before continuing any further, let me explain the relevant pieces of this diagram, which itself contains a wealth of information. \u00a0First, the axes. \u00a0The vertical Y-axis is the magnitude axis, where objects with smaller magnitudes correspond to higher brightnesses. \u00a0If this is confusing to you, don&#8217;t worry, as you&#8217;re most surely not alone. \u00a0Blame for the Magnitude System falls squarely on the shoulders of <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hipparchus\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">Hipparchus</a> (the guy who created it), Norman Robert Pogson (for formalizing it), and the human eye (for perceiving brightness logarithmically). \u00a0The horizontal axis is the <b>color</b>\u00a0axis, where the color for a given star corresponds to the numerical difference between its B and V-band magnitudes.</p>\n<p><i><br />\n</i><i>Hey, whoa Nick, what in the hell are you talking about when you refer to the B and V-bands?!</i></p>\n<p>Hmm&#8230; well, let&#8217;s back it up a little bit more and talk briefly about filters and the electromagnetic (EM) spectrum. \u00a0For more on the latter, see <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-light-you-magnificent-bastard/\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">the previous Science Sunday post</a>.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_98\" style=\"width: 508px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/em_spectrum.gif\"><img class=\"size-full wp-image-98\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/em_spectrum.gif?resize=508%2C293\" alt=\"Light of all types. It's really kinda crazy that we can only actually perceive so little of it!\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Light of all types. It&#8217;s really kinda crazy that we can only actually perceive so little of it!</figcaption></figure>\n<figure style=\"width: 320px\" class=\"wp-caption alignleft\"><img src=\"https://images-blogger-opensocial.googleusercontent.com/gadgets/proxy?url=http%3A%2F%2Foutreach.atnf.csiro.au%2Feducation%2Fsenior%2Fastrophysics%2Fimages%2Fphotometry%2FUBVRIpassbands.gif&amp;container=blogger&amp;gadget=a&amp;rewriteMime=image%2F*\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"196\" border=\"0\" data-orig-src=\"http://outreach.atnf.csiro.au/education/senior/astrophysics/images/photometry/UBVRIpassbands.gif\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Graphical representation of UBVRI filters with wavelength</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Light doesn&#8217;t just come in one flavor to fit every palette. \u00a0There&#8217;s a whole selection of varieties of light called the EM spectrum, where these different varieties are separated by how long their individual wavelengths are. \u00a0These wavelengths range from light waves longer than your physical height (meters), on down to waves shorter than the physical &#8220;size&#8221; of a single electron (<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Femtometer\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">femtometers</a>), and beyond in both directions. \u00a0When we want to isolate a specific section of this massive range, we use some material to block out everything else, with that material being called a <a href=\"http://img.directindustry.com/images_di/photo-g/interference-optical-filter-53152.jpg\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">filter</a>. \u00a0A filter for, as an example, visible light works in much the same way that a security checkpoint does at the airport, letting in the legitimate passengers (the visible light), and (hopefully) screening out bombs and terrorists (an overdramatic characterization of light outside the visible wavelengths).<br />\nIf we want to then break up visible light into colors that we (more or less) recognize as familiar, we use narrower filters. \u00a0<a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UBV_photometric_system\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">One particular set of filters</a> does this (well really, every set of optical filters does this), and assigns to different wavelength ranges the letters U, B, V, R, and I (near-Ultraviolet, Blue, Yellow-Green, Red, near Infrared respectively). \u00a0Note that V, which stands essentially for &#8220;visible&#8221;, encompasses &#8220;yellow-green&#8221; because our eyes are most sensitive to yellow-green light as opposed to everything else. \u00a0With all that said, when I refer to B and V-bands, I&#8217;m referring to the wavelength ranges falling within the B and V filters.</p>\n<figure style=\"width: 320px\" class=\"wp-caption alignright\"><img src=\"https://images-blogger-opensocial.googleusercontent.com/gadgets/proxy?url=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ucolick.org%2F~bolte%2FAY4_00%2Fweek2%2Fstellar_temps1.gif&amp;container=blogger&amp;gadget=a&amp;rewriteMime=image%2F*\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"205\" border=\"0\" data-orig-src=\"http://www.ucolick.org/~bolte/AY4_00/week2/stellar_temps1.gif\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">A great example of a &#8220;blue&#8221; star dominating the brightness in the blue, and a &#8220;red&#8221; star dominating in the red while still being dimmer than the &#8220;blue&#8221; star</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Right. So, something appears &#8220;bluer&#8221; if it&#8217;s brighter in the blue filter than it is in the yellow-green filter. \u00a0This will make its B-V color negative, where more-negative means more-blue (mathematically, if it&#8217;s brighter in B than in V, then the object&#8217;s B magnitude will be smaller than the object&#8217;s V magnitude. \u00a0When you subtract the two, you&#8217;ll get a negative number. \u00a0Like 1 &#8211; 2 = -1). \u00a0On the contrary, if it is instead brighter in the yellow-green than it is in the blue, then we say that the object is &#8220;redder&#8221;, and it will have a positive B-V color. \u00a0That&#8217;s because its peak brightness occurs at longer wavelengths, with the longest of the visible wavelengths being in the red. \u00a0It&#8217;s all relative, much like temperature. \u00a0We&#8217;d say that a 90-degree day is &#8220;cooler&#8221; than a 110-degree day, even though 90 degrees is nowhere near being actually &#8220;cold&#8221; by our standards.</p>\n<p>Anyway, we organize stars in this way (magnitude vs. color) because stars tend to fall along pretty distinct tracks when we do. \u00a0What do I mean by that? \u00a0Well, check these labels out here in relation to where each subtype falls on the HR diagram (labeled HR diagram below this section; rest assured that each of these will have its own lengthy, long-winded blog post in the future):</p>\n<p class=\"separator\"><a href=\"http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Solar_internal_structure.svg/280px-Solar_internal_structure.svg.png\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://images-blogger-opensocial.googleusercontent.com/gadgets/proxy?url=http%3A%2F%2Fupload.wikimedia.org%2Fwikipedia%2Fcommons%2Fthumb%2F8%2F89%2FSolar_internal_structure.svg%2F280px-Solar_internal_structure.svg.png&amp;container=blogger&amp;gadget=a&amp;rewriteMime=image%2F*\" alt=\"\" border=\"0\" data-orig-src=\"http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Solar_internal_structure.svg/280px-Solar_internal_structure.svg.png\" /></a></p>\n<ul>\n<li><b>The Main Sequence:</b> Where stars of all types reside after their initial birth. \u00a0Stars on the Main Sequence will be fusing hydrogen into helium (by way of either <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton_proton_chain\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">the p-p chain</a> or <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CNO_cycle\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">the CNO cycle</a>) in their cores, much like <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-the-stars-part-i-introduction-to-shiny-things/\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">our own Sun</a>. \u00a0Roughly about 10% of a star&#8217;s initial mass will be involved in fusion over its lifetime as a Main Sequence star, with 0.7% of that mass being converted into energy. \u00a0The length of this lifetime can be (roughly) derived by calculating the amount of mass that&#8217;s converted into energy and dividing it by the energy output by the star per year. \u00a0For the Sun, we have its *rough* initial mass of 2 x 10^30 kg. \u00a010% of that is 2 x 10^29 kg, and 0.7% \u00a0of THAT is \u00a01.4 x 10^27 kg. \u00a0If you convert mass into energy using that famous equation of Einstein&#8217;s: E = mc^2, you find that over the Sun&#8217;s main sequence lifetime, it&#8217;ll produce 1.26 x 10^44 Joules of energy. \u00a0For reference, <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energy_in_the_United_States\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">the average energy consumption per year in the United States is 335.9 million BTUs per person</a>, which works out to about 3.54 x 10^11 Joules per person. \u00a0Since the US has roughly 314 million people, this rounds out to an annual energy consumption rate of about 1 x 10^20 Joules (or roughly a hundred billion billion Joules) per year. \u00a0If the Sun only produced as much energy as the United States consumed per year, it&#8217;d last for roughly 10^24 years, or 1 million billion billion years. \u00a0However the Sun produces about 4 x 10^26 Joules <b><i><u>per second</u></i></b> (4 million times the amount of energy we consume in a year). \u00a0This means about 1.26 x 10^34 Joules per year!. \u00a0If we divide the total energy production of the Sun in its lifetime (1.26 x 10^44 J) by this rate (1.26 x 10^34 J/year), we get out that the Sun should last about 10^10 years, or 10 billion years.</li>\n</ul>\n<ul>\n<li><b>The Subgiants: </b>Stars become subgiants when a) they are less than ~8 times as massive as our own Sun, <b><u>and</u></b> b) they have already run out of hydrogen fuel in their cores or are at the tail end of their hydrogen fusion. \u00a0The difference in b) is determined by how massive the star is initially. \u00a0In fact, what you&#8217;ll find out is that everything regarding a star&#8217;s evolution is determined by its initial mass and composition, but I digress. \u00a0If a star&#8217;s mass is above, say, a few solar masses, then its core will be depleted of hydrogen (filled now with inert helium) and contract. \u00a0This contraction will increase the temperature and density of the hydrogen gas around the helium core, and initiate hydrogen fusion in a thin shell around that core. \u00a0If instead a star has less than a few solar masses, it will forego an abrupt shutting-off of core fusion in lieu of a more gradual slow-down of core fusion, with some slower contraction of the core. \u00a0Overall, stars are in this phase before they jump into the Red Giant Branch.</li>\n</ul>\n<ul>\n<li><b><a href=\"http://outreach.atnf.csiro.au/education/senior/astrophysics/images/stellarevolution/hshellburningsml.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://images-blogger-opensocial.googleusercontent.com/gadgets/proxy?url=http%3A%2F%2Foutreach.atnf.csiro.au%2Feducation%2Fsenior%2Fastrophysics%2Fimages%2Fstellarevolution%2Fhshellburningsml.jpg&amp;container=blogger&amp;gadget=a&amp;rewriteMime=image%2F*\" alt=\"\" width=\"304\" height=\"320\" border=\"0\" data-orig-src=\"http://outreach.atnf.csiro.au/education/senior/astrophysics/images/stellarevolution/hshellburningsml.jpg\" /></a>The Red Giants: </b>As the name implies, these stars are red and physically gigantic in size. \u00a0Not exactly rocket science right there. \u00a0Stars become red giants when they evolve out of the subgiant phase. \u00a0They&#8217;re earnestly fusing hydrogen in a thin shell around their inert helium cores, and fusing it at a higher temperature than they were in their Main Sequence phase. \u00a0Because the efficiency of hydrogen fusion is strongly dependent on temperature (~T^4, though not nearly as strongly dependent as helium fusion is!), the hydrogen-fusing shell outputs more energy than the hydrogen core used to. \u00a0This excess of energy pumps up the star&#8217;s luminosity by a factor of &gt;1,000! \u00a0This extra bit of luminosity makes the star really bloated and extends the hydrogen envelope of the star as well. \u00a0Because of the extended atmosphere, you have more light going over more surface area and over longer distances. \u00a0Due to many scatterings with atoms in the envelope over this distance, as well as something magnificent called convection, the emergent light is less-energetic than the light that emerges from the surface of Main Sequence stars. Thus, the star appears cooler, and redder. \u00a0Red star + big star = Red Giant!</li>\n<li><strong>The Horizontal Branch:\u00a0</strong>As the hydrogen shell in a red giant star continues to fuse, the helium products of that fusion get dumped onto the core of the star, which then contracts and heats up.\u00a0When the core temperature reaches 10^8 K (100 MILLION!), the helium core will ignite almost all at once, in what&#8217;s known as <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium_flash\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">the helium flash</a>. During this time, the hydrogen-fusing shell is still in existence on top of the helium-fusing core. \u00a0However, when the core ignites, it will expand and thus greatly expand the hydrogen shell sitting above it, diminishing the fusing efficiency of that shell. \u00a0This decrease in luminosity will cause the size of the star to plummet (but not down to Main Sequence sizes). \u00a0As the star evolves through the Horizontal Branch, it will essentially maintain the same luminosity while growing progressively hotter (for reasons which I forget and are in my Stellar Interiors notes in my office). \u00a0This is the reason why it&#8217;s called the <i>horizontal</i>\u00a0branch; stars on this branch all have the same luminosity (again, essentially), but vary widely in temperature.</li>\n<li><strong><a href=\"http://www.noao.edu/outreach/press/pr03/images/agb_cutaway_big.gif\"><img class=\"alignright\" src=\"https://images-blogger-opensocial.googleusercontent.com/gadgets/proxy?url=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.noao.edu%2Foutreach%2Fpress%2Fpr03%2Fimages%2Fagb_cutaway_big.gif&amp;container=blogger&amp;gadget=a&amp;rewriteMime=image%2F*\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"213\" border=\"0\" data-orig-src=\"http://www.noao.edu/outreach/press/pr03/images/agb_cutaway_big.gif\" /></a>The Asymptotic Giants:\u00a0</strong>As the star moves through helium fusion on the horizontal branch, it will of course drop the product of helium fusion deeper into its core. \u00a0In this case, the product is carbon. \u00a0So, carbon builds up in the core, and the helium core grows outward on top of the new carbon core. \u00a0Much like how the helium core was inert on the Red Giant branch, here the carbon core will be inert as it&#8217;s not hot enough to ignite. \u00a0Outside of the carbon core, you&#8217;ll have your helium shell fusing (more on this in a sec), and on top of THAT you have the hydrogen shell still plugging away. \u00a0When it&#8217;s in this <b>double-shell burning</b>\u00a0phase, it&#8217;s officially on the Asymptotic Giant branch. \u00a0This is the phase of utmost interest to me, because it&#8217;s a very dynamic phase of stellar evolution. \u00a0For one, the star is so bright and so extended that it&#8217;s actually losing a fair amount of its envelope via <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_wind\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">stellar winds</a> and <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_pulsations\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">stellar pulsation</a>. \u00a0This mass loss rate is sometimes as high as 10^(-4) solar masses per year (sometimes even higher!). \u00a0For reference, a solar mass is 2 x 10^33 grams, so 10^(-4) of that would STILL be like 2 x 10^29 grams every year. \u00a0EVERY YEAR! \u00a0That&#8217;s bananas! (<a href=\"http://youtu.be/QpgxnLlOt34\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">B-A-N-A-N-A-S!</a>) \u00a0For reference, the Earth is roughly 6 x 10^27 grams in mass. \u00a0This means that every year, these stars blow off more than 50 Earth&#8217;s worth of mass, just because they&#8217;re crazy like that. \u00a0The second dynamic bit is the fact that once the star is in this phase, it never quite reaches an equilibrium point again where it maintains some stable shape. \u00a0Its hydrogen outer shell dominates the energy output and increases the temperature and mass of the inner helium shell. \u00a0When enough matter and temperature have built up in the inner helium shell, it goes through a mini helium flash which we call a thermal pulse, and the star increases in brightness and size for a brief amount of time. \u00a0During this pulse outward, the hydrogen shell expands and effectively shuts off, until the helium shell dies down and contracts again. \u00a0After the contraction the hydrogen shell begins fusing again and the cycle restarts. \u00a0The time lag between successive pulses is on the order of 1,000 years (&#8220;on the order of&#8221; is astronomer-speak for &#8220;somewhere between one half of this number and 5 times this number&#8221;). \u00a0There&#8217;s another, short-term pulsation mechanism called the <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%9A_mechanism\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">K-mechanism</a>, but I&#8217;ll leave that for another time.\n<p><a href=\"http://cse.ssl.berkeley.edu/bmendez/ay10/2000/cycle/whitedwarf.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft\" src=\"https://images-blogger-opensocial.googleusercontent.com/gadgets/proxy?url=http%3A%2F%2Fcse.ssl.berkeley.edu%2Fbmendez%2Fay10%2F2000%2Fcycle%2Fwhitedwarf.jpg&amp;container=blogger&amp;gadget=a&amp;rewriteMime=image%2F*\" alt=\"\" width=\"320\" height=\"258\" border=\"0\" data-orig-src=\"http://cse.ssl.berkeley.edu/bmendez/ay10/2000/cycle/whitedwarf.jpg\" /></a></li>\n<li><strong>The White Dwarfs:</strong>\u00a0<b> </b>The Asymptotic Giant branch represents the last phase of stellar evolution where you can still call the object a star. \u00a0After this point, the star will have puffed off its envelope through stellar winds and pulsations, leaving behind the white-hot carbon/oxygen core in the center of a brightly-shining <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_nebula\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">planetary nebula</a>. \u00a0This core is what we call the white dwarf. \u00a0This object is as hot as some of the hottest starts (recall, it came from the hot interior of an Asymptotic Giant) and has a mass comparable to our own Sun&#8217;s. However its size is about that of the Earth&#8217;s. \u00a0As you might imagine, this combination makes it a very dense object. \u00a0Simply based off of the information that I&#8217;ve given you here, you can derive just how dense this is (well, approximately anyway). \u00a0Let&#8217;s say that our white dwarf has a mass M of about 1 solar mass (2 x 10^33 grams), and a radius R equal to the Earth&#8217;s radius (about 6.4 x 10^3 km, so 6.4 x 10^8 cm). \u00a0First, we calculate the volume: V = (4/3)\u00a0\u03c0 R^3 = (4/3)\u00a0\u03c0 (6.4 x 10^8 cm)^3 = 1.1 x 10^27 cm^3. \u00a0Average density is defined as M/V, so the density of this white dwarf will be (2 x 10^33 g)/(1.1 x 10^27 cm^3) = 1.83 x 10^6 grams per cubic centimeter! \u00a0That is FREAKING absurd! \u00a0A cubic centimeter would be like the size of your pinky, halfway above the top knuckle. \u00a0In that small volume, a white dwarf would pack in 1.83 x 10^6 grams (or 1.83 x 10^3 kg, or 1.83 MEGAGRAMS!) of matter! \u00a0That&#8217;s nearly a third of the Earth&#8217;s mass on tip of your finger. \u00a0Crazy. \u00a0There&#8217;s other crazy stuff with white dwarfs, but I&#8217;ll get to that another time.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>They&#8217;re all grouped in these tracks on the HR diagram, all in relation to their temperatures (color) and brightnesses (magnitude).</p>\n<table class=\"tr-caption-container\" cellspacing=\"0\" cellpadding=\"0\" align=\"center\">\n<tbody>\n<tr>\n<td><a href=\"http://www.pa.uky.edu/~shlosman/anim/HRdiaM15.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"https://images-blogger-opensocial.googleusercontent.com/gadgets/proxy?url=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.pa.uky.edu%2F~shlosman%2Fanim%2FHRdiaM15.jpg&amp;container=blogger&amp;gadget=a&amp;rewriteMime=image%2F*\" alt=\"\" width=\"640\" height=\"406\" border=\"0\" data-orig-src=\"http://www.pa.uky.edu/~shlosman/anim/HRdiaM15.jpg\" /></a></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td class=\"tr-caption\">\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\"><i>An HR diagram depicting all the above-mentioned populations and more, with the source stellar cluster next to it. \u00a0For reference, this is M15, a globular cluster.</i></p>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody>\n</table>\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n<p>Ok so this post is going to be longer than I had originally anticipated, so I&#8217;m going to do the rest in later installments. \u00a0This should be enough now to get your astronomy juices flowing again though. \u00a0Note that there are hundreds of subtypes of stars, and I&#8217;ll try to get to those in the future. \u00a0Until next time!</p>\n<p><span style=\"color: #222222; font-family: Arial, Tahoma, Helvetica, FreeSans, sans-serif;\">*</span><i>Because I&#8217;m really just that lazy, all of my facts and figures either come from my faulty memory or from somewhere on the internets. \u00a0Conversions and calculations were done almost entirely on my iPhone, and may be a little (or a lot) off due to rounding and general laziness. \u00a0This is certainly not an ultimately-reliable source for facts and figures, just an astronomer rambling about stuff that gets him excited. \u00a0Enjoy for your own pleasure, but cite at your own peril! \u00a0In fact, seriously, don&#8217;t cite at all. \u00a0If you&#8217;re citing this as a source, you need some serious serious help.</i></p>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-18T02:47:05", "date_gmt": "2012-07-21T02:27:26", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=95", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=95", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:featuredmedia": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media/96", "embeddable": true}], "replies": [{"href": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=95"}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>Oh snap! It&#8217;s that time again! On the first Science Sunday, I discussed the Sun. \u00a0After that, I talked about charged particles and light. \u00a0The goal there was to talk about light and charged particles in order to build up the machinery for this and later blog posts, where I hope to talk about all [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [13, 31, 2, 16, 23, 24], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=95"}, "id": 95, "title": {"rendered": "Science Sunday: The Stars &#8211; Part II: All Types of Shiny Things"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-18T02:47:05", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "science-sunday-the-stars-part-ii-all-types-of-shiny-things", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 78, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-light-you-magnificent-bastard/", "date": "2012-06-24T02:12:39", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<figure id=\"attachment_79\" style=\"width: 300px\" class=\"wp-caption alignleft\"><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves1.gif\"><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-79\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves1.gif?resize=300%2C225\" alt=\"An animated gif showing a prism dispersing visible, white light into its color components: red, orange, yellow, green, blue, indigo (seriously? indigo?), and violet\" srcset=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves1.gif?resize=300%2C225 300w, http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves1.gif?resize=135%2C100 135w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">A prism dispersing visible, white light into its color components: red, orange, yellow, green, blue, indigo (seriously? indigo?), and violet</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Well now, it sure has been a while, hasn&#8217;t it? \u00a0It&#8217;s about time that I got back to this, no?</p>\n<p>Last time on Science Sunday,\u00a0<a href=\"http://nhuntwalker.blogspot.com/2012/01/science-sunday-stars-part-i.html\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">I discussed the Sun</a>. \u00a0I didn&#8217;t discuss it in it&#8217;s entirety though, because let&#8217;s face it, there&#8217;s enough going on with the Sun to warrant its own blog. \u00a0In fact, it already has it&#8217;s own\u00a0<a href=\"http://www.springerlink.com/content/0038-0938\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">Scientific Journal</a>! \u00a0Nevertheless, I did a quick overview of how it stays up against the constant threat of gravity without really discussing much of any of the interior physics, and I&#8217;m going to just continue to leave that as an open question for some later date.</p>\n<p>What I originally wanted to do today was move on toward discussing larger populations of stars beyond our own Sun and those like it (and believe me, there are many). \u00a0It seemed like the next logical step to me: start with one really well known star, and use that as a jumping-off point to talk about stars in general. \u00a0However, about one-tenth (if even THAT much) of the way through writing that post, I hit what I perceive to be a massive roadblock. \u00a0The way that astronomers categorize stars has everything to do with the light that they give off, and it&#8217;s really pretty damn stupid to talk about this categorization and go onward from there without actually talking about what light IS! \u00a0It&#8217;s somewhat like saying &#8220;hey, let&#8217;s talk about all of the different types of cars there are, basing the types on the engines they house,&#8221; with you all like &#8220;hey that&#8217;s great and all, but what in the hell is an engine?&#8221;</p>\n<p>I&#8217;m supposed to be educating here right? \u00a0Right! \u00a0So let&#8217;s get on with the edumacation* regarding that most magnificently brilliant of things in the Universe: the phenomenon of light! \u00a0The most blindingly magnificent of bastards!</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_80\" style=\"width: 263px\" class=\"wp-caption alignright\"><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/basicwavesjavafigure1.jpg\"><img class=\"size-full wp-image-80\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/basicwavesjavafigure1.jpg?resize=263%2C187\" alt=\"What? What is this? This is nonsense! Dammit man, put this into plain English!\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">What? What is this? This is nonsense! Dammit man, put this into plain English!</figcaption></figure>\n<p>So the question is: what is light? \u00a0The short(ish), somewhat confusing answer is that light is the manifestation of electric and magnetic waves propagating in phase with one another, with their amplitudes directed perpendicularly to one another as well as their direction of motion, through space, and is also a massless particle containing all of the energy of those aforementioned waves in one discrete little packet moving at the same speed as those waves. \u00a0In other words, <b><i>the short answer is\u00a0complete nonsense</i></b>. \u00a0Even to me! And I wrote it! \u00a0True understanding comes from the long answer, so let&#8217;s instead get neck-deep into that long answer. \u00a0To begin with, <i>in order to understand the nature of light you have to know a little bit about charged particles</i>.</p>\n<p><b>Hey Electron, Why Must You be So Negative?</b></p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_81\" style=\"width: 233px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/electric-lines-negative-charge.jpeg\"><img class=\"size-full wp-image-81\" src=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/electric-lines-negative-charge.jpeg?resize=233%2C251\" alt=\"An electron, which is negatively-charged, shown with electric field lines pointing towards it. You don't know 'bout that yet. We'll get to that later on in our program\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">An electron, which is negatively-charged, shown with electric field lines pointing towards it. You don&#8217;t know &#8217;bout that yet. We&#8217;ll get to that later on in our program</figcaption></figure>\n<p>I had used the electron in <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/science-sunday-the-stars-part-i-introduction-to-shiny-things/\">the previous Science Sunday post</a> to talk about fusion without really talking all that much about the electron itself, so here we go.\u00a0The electron is your basic charged particle. \u00a0So basic in fact that fundamental units of charge are based upon that of the electron. \u00a0I really wish that I could give an adequate description of what a &#8220;charge&#8221; is, but it&#8217;s a lot like defining &#8220;mass&#8221;. \u00a0I&#8217;ll put it like this: in a similar way that mass is how much physical &#8220;stuff&#8221; an object possesses, charge is how much <b>electric</b>\u00a0&#8220;stuff&#8221; an object possesses.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_82\" style=\"width: 300px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/coulomb2.jpg\"><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-82\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/coulomb2.jpg?resize=300%2C231\" alt=\"Top (attraction): Q1 - Man, my outlook is so negative! Q2 - Come closer and let me add some positivity to your life. In fact, the more negative you are, the more that I want you next to me! Q1 - I can dig it. Bottom (repulsion): Q1 - Man, my outlook is so negative! Q2 - Dude, everything's already negative for me. Get away, I don't need yours too. Q1 - Yeah...well...I don't want you around either! Q2 - Fine! Q1 - FINE THEN!\" srcset=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/coulomb2.jpg?resize=300%2C231 300w, http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/coulomb2.jpg?w=360 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Top (attraction): Q1 &#8211; Man, my outlook is so negative! Q2 &#8211; Come closer and let me add some positivity to your life. In fact, the more negative you are, the more that I want you next to me! Q1 &#8211; I can dig it.<br />Bottom (repulsion): Q1 &#8211; Man, my outlook is so negative! Q2 &#8211; Dude, everything&#8217;s already negative for me. Get away, I don&#8217;t need yours too. Q1 &#8211; Yeah&#8230;well&#8230;I don&#8217;t want you around either! Q2 &#8211; Fine! Q1 &#8211; FINE THEN!</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Much <i>unlike</i> mass, charges come in two varieties: positive (+) and negative (-). \u00a0The way in which this matters is that similar charges (i.e. (+)(+) or (-)(-)) repel each other, while opposing charges ((+)(-)) attract each other. \u00a0This repulsion/attraction manifests as an electric force (push or pull) between the two charges. Regardless of whether it&#8217;s repulsion or attraction, the strength of the force between the two charges increases as the amount (more negative or more positive) of either charge (charges q1 and q2) increases. \u00a0It also decreases as they get further apart.</p>\n<p><b>You Turn Me On With Your Electric Field</b></p>\n<p><b></b>Every electron existing somewhere sets up what&#8217;s called an <b>electric field </b>(from here on, called the E-field)<b>. \u00a0</b>The way to think about this is that the E-field is the area of influence of that electron. \u00a0If you had a stronger electric charge (say, TWO electrons *gasp*), then together they&#8217;d set up a stronger E-field (twice as strong, in fact). \u00a0Electric fields are typically shown in pictures as lines radiating away from some central charge, with arrowheads pointing toward the charge if it&#8217;s negative and away from the charge if it&#8217;s positive. \u00a0Therefore, a negative and positive charge together (in perfect harmony) set up an E-field like the one shown in the picture to the left. \u00a0For the sake of simplicity for later let&#8217;s imagine that \u00a0charges q1(+) and q2(-) are nailed in place, such that they set up an unchanging E-field. \u00a0This field, has some strength, <i>E</i>, based on the charges that set it up. \u00a0Ok. \u00a0That framework is set.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_83\" style=\"width: 289px\" class=\"wp-caption alignleft\"><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/espaciociencia.com_.wp-content.uploads.2009.10.dipol-thumb.jpg\"><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-83\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/espaciociencia.com_.wp-content.uploads.2009.10.dipol-thumb.jpg?resize=289%2C300\" alt=\"Oh, how pretty\" srcset=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/espaciociencia.com_.wp-content.uploads.2009.10.dipol-thumb.jpg?resize=289%2C300 289w, http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/espaciociencia.com_.wp-content.uploads.2009.10.dipol-thumb.jpg?w=377 377w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 289px) 100vw, 289px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Oh, how pretty</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Now, if you can, stretch your imagination to place a freely-moving third charge (q3 (+)) within our beloved E-field. \u00a0q3 experiences a force based on the strength of the E-field (surprised?), and the strength of q3, where the strength of the force on q3 increases if either the strength of the charge or the strength of the field increases. \u00a0The glorious thing about the field is that when q3 is placed within this field, it&#8217;ll move in whatever direction the field is pointing. \u00a0The amount of this movement, again, is dictated by the force it experiences within the field (and curiously enough, not the mass of the charged object). \u00a0Lovely.</p>\n<p><b>Word, You&#8217;re Not Confused Yet? Challenge Accepted</b></p>\n<p>Now it&#8217;s time to talk about magnetic fields (from here on, called B-fields. \u00a0Don&#8217;t ask me why they chose &#8220;B&#8221;), and I tell you now that I hope I don&#8217;t screw it up. \u00a0It&#8217;s actually a lot easier to understand if you understand the mathematics, but bleh. \u00a0Whatever.</p>\n<p>Above, I talked about two charges creating a static (i.e. unmoving, unchanging) E-field. \u00a0 Well, if the strength or the direction (or both) of the E-field changes, then something kinda crazy happens: the changing E-field creates a B-field! \u00a0The B-field that&#8217;s created interacts with your charged particles in a somewhat different way than your static E-field, but the weird thing about it is that at its base the E-field and the B-field are just two parts of the same phenomenon of <b>electromagnetism</b>. The way to think about it that makes the most sense is that <i>magnetic fields are emergent properties of electric fields when electric fields can&#8217;t keep still</i>. \u00a0An analogy that I find useful is that of a fan with lights on its fins (below). \u00a0When the fan is static, all that you see is lights on one of the fins. \u00a0However, when the fan is turned on and the fins start to spin, a pattern emerges that wasn&#8217;t there before. \u00a0The underlying structure of the fan with lights on the fins is the same, but in motion you get what appears to be a different phenomenon!</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/fanstationary.jpg\"><img class=\"alignleft wp-image-85\" src=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/fanstationary.jpg?resize=235%2C235\" alt=\"fanstationary\" srcset=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/fanstationary.jpg?w=300 300w, http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/fanstationary.jpg?resize=150%2C150 150w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 235px) 100vw, 235px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><a href=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/fanmotion.gif\"><img class=\"size-full wp-image-84 alignleft\" src=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/fanmotion.gif?resize=170%2C235\" alt=\"fanmotion\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<div style=\"clear: both;\"></div>\n<p>Now that I see it, this is an almost perfect analogy, haha. \u00a0The same thing happens between electric and magnetic fields! \u00a0When you start spinning, shaking, oscillating (this becomes REALLY IMPORTANT), or otherwise changing E-fields, B-fields emerge. \u00a0B-fields can appear to be an entirely different phenomenon, but they&#8217;re really only the product of changing E-fields!</p>\n<p><b>And Now I Drop The Bomb on Your Brain Meats</b></p>\n<p>If you change an E-field at a constant rate, you set up a static B-field. \u00a0Now, if you change the RATE at which you&#8217;re changing your E-field, you change the B-field that you generate. \u00a0If you then change the rate at which you&#8217;re changing your B-field, you set up an E-field! \u00a0What?! YES! It&#8217;s true! <i>A changing E-field produces a changing B-field, and a changing B-field produces a changing E-field!</i>\u00a0 Example in the figure below.</p>\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/image002-06-12-2009.gif\"><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-89 aligncenter\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/image002-06-12-2009.gif?resize=300%2C160\" alt=\"Honestly, it doesn't matter at this point which is the E-field and which is the B-field. The point is that changing fields change each other\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n<p>Honestly, it doesn&#8217;t matter at this point which is the\u00a0E-field and which is the B-field. The point is that\u00a0changing fields change each other</p>\n<p>At this point, if by some stroke of luck or extreme patience you&#8217;re still reading this blog post, you&#8217;re probably wondering to yourself, what does ANY of this have to do with light? \u00a0Well, funny you should ask. \u00a0That animated gif above is actually a representation of the oscillating E-fields and B-fields that compose a wave of light. \u00a0The sizes of the arrows represent the strength of each field at a given point in time, and the direction of each arrow is&#8230;well&#8230;the direction of each field at any given point in time. \u00a0As the fields oscillate back and forth, they do so with some physical length for the completion of one oscillation cycle called a <b>wavelength</b>. \u00a0The faster the <b>frequency </b>of\u00a0oscillation, the shorter the wavelength, and vice versa. \u00a0Additionally, the shorter the wavelength, the higher the energy of the light being produced.</p>\n<p>As you can probably imagine, there&#8217;s a whole range of wavelengths that a given wave of light can possess. \u00a0Perhaps&#8230;perhaps even a whole <i>spectrum</i> of wavelengths for these\u00a0<i>electromagnetic</i>\u00a0waves. \u00a0We should probably give a name to that. \u00a0How about the <i>electromagnetic spectrum?</i></p>\n<p><a href=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/spectrum.gif\"><img class=\"alignleft size-medium wp-image-90\" src=\"http://i0.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2012/06/spectrum.gif?resize=300%2C200\" alt=\"spectrum\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>Interesting thing here is that the light that we see, the visible light (red through violet), represents only a veeeeeeery small portion (greatly exaggerated here) of the total amount of light present in the Universe. \u00a0Light ranges from the Radio wavelengths (at the lowest energy end) up through and beyond Gamma Rays, and everywhere in between. \u00a0Different objects/phenomena produce different types of light (a subject that we&#8217;ll delve into in other Science Sundays), and different processes produce different intensities of that light.</p>\n<p>As astronomers, light is our tool. \u00a0It is how we measure the Universe and what we use to model just what in the hell might be going on out there. \u00a0As I talk more and more about astronomy and the stuff that we see out in the Universe, I&#8217;ll reference light as sort of just this thing that shows us what&#8217;s out there. \u00a0But, we shouldn&#8217;t ever lose the knowledge of what produces light in the first place and what it&#8217;s composed of. \u00a0It matters! \u00a0Hmmm&#8230;maybe I should do a post sometime about the different ways in which light is actually produced in the Universe. \u00a0Maybe I will. \u00a0But not next time!</p>\n<p>Next time, star stuff!</p>\n<p><span style=\"color: #222222; font-family: Arial, Tahoma, Helvetica, FreeSans, sans-serif; font-size: small;\"><br />\n</span><span style=\"color: #222222; />\n</span><span style=\"color: #222222; font-family: Arial, Tahoma, Helvetica, FreeSans, sans-serif;\">*<i>Beware, we about to get all technical n&#8217; whatnot up in here. \u00a0I&#8217;m going to throw an equation or two at you because I expect you to be adults that won&#8217;t crap their pants at numbers and other math on the screen. \u00a0</i></span><i>Because I&#8217;m on vacation, all of my facts and figures either come from my faulty memory or from somewhere on the internets (read: Wikipedia and Google). \u00a0Any conversions and calculations that may have been done were done almost Inside the Parthenon, there&#8217;s a small art exhibit in two parts: one from the personal collection of this dude who loved Tennessee from afar, and one that I suspect is a rotating exhibit.</p>\n<p>Currently on rotation is an exhibit by a local artist Juan Pont Lezica, called <a href=\"http://www.nashville.gov/Parthenon/Galleries/Galleries-East.asp\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">Artwork Among Us</a>, that displays classic works of art reinterpreted through modern eyes using modern models to take the places of classic figures. One model in particular had a tattoo on her back in full Greek of this quote by Fyodor Dostoyevsky from his book Notes from Underground:</p>\n<p>\u201cYou see, gentlemen, reason is an excellent thing, there\u2019s no disputing that, but reason is nothing but reason and satisfies only the rational side of man\u2019s nature, while will is a manifestation of the whole life, that is, of the whole human life including reason and all the impulses. And although our life, in this manifestation of it, is often worthless, yet it is life and not simply extracting square roots. Here I, for instance, quite naturally want to live, in order to satisfy all my capacities for life, and not simply my capacity for reasoning, that is, not simply one twentieth of my capacity for life. What does reason know? Reason only knows what it has succeeded in learning (some things, perhaps, it will never learn; this is a poor comfort, but why not say so frankly?) and human nature acts as a whole, with everything that is in it, consciously or unconsciously, and, even if it goes wrong, it lives.&#8221;</p>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-18T02:12:29", "date_gmt": "2012-06-22T02:08:44", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/categories?post=75", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "category"}, {"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/tags?post=75", "embeddable": true, "taxonomy": "post_tag"}], "curies": [{"href": "https://api.w.org/{rel}", "name": "wp", "templated": true}], "self": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/75"}], "version-history": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts/75/revisions"}], "collection": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/posts"}], "wp:attachment": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/media?parent=75"}], "replies": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/comments?post=75", "embeddable": true}], "author": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/users/1", "embeddable": true}], "about": [{"href": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/wp-json/wp/v2/types/post"}]}, "excerpt": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>I happened upon this quote during my current stay in Nashville at the Parthenon. Currently on rotation is an exhibit by a [&hellip;]</p>\n"}, "categories": [1], "tags": [26, 37, 38, 39], "guid": {"rendered": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/?p=75"}, "id": 75, "title": {"rendered": "Interesting Quote"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-18T02:12:29", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "interesting-quote", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 0, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/impostor-syndrome-redux/", "date": "2012-06-20T02:07:05", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p>In response to a previous post of mine about <a href=\"http://rationalwhimsy.com/experiences-with-impostor-syndrome/\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">my own experiences with the impostor phenomenon</a>, a friend of mine, Joji Kojima, wrote this awesomeness that follows. \u00a0I definitely think it&#8217;s worth reading, and I&#8217;m still thinking about a worthy response to his wrote. \u00a0Anyway, here it is:</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<blockquote><p><span style=\"color: #333333; font-family: 'lucida grande', tahoma, verdana, arial, sans-serif;\">Nick, I appreciate your honesty in sharing your perspective at this stage of your experience. I had some preliminary thoughts to share about what might be called the &#8220;impostor complex&#8221; which you have described.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"color: #333333; font-family: 'lucida grande', tahoma, verdana, arial, sans-serif;\">I recently worked as a lab</span><span class=\"text_exposed_show\" style=\"color: #333333; font-family: 'lucida grande', tahoma, verdana, arial, sans-serif;\">\u00a0tech for six months, and I can say that if I never again work in such environs I will not miss it much. The reason is that one feels very small in the face of science. The compromise we make, as scientists, is that we can see ourselves as relevant to a given project, and if we can convince ourselves that that project is relevant to capital S science, then we can go on about our lives convinced that we are also relevant even as small cogs in a big machine. However, for those who are given to skepticism, simply the fact that our project exists, is endorsed by an enthusiastic PI, and is receiving funding sufficient to maintain the project is often not enough to convince us that said project is in fact relevant.</p>\n<p>As the world becomes more complex, particularly in fields such as yours which rely on huge amounts of data collection and analysis, where the limiting factor in many experiments is likely related to the organization of large amounts of data, a given person is likely to be so specialized that they feel overwhelmed, as you describe, by the huge amount that they don&#8217;t know. If this arrangement of division of labor works, we can rest assured that our own intellectual output is feeding into a kind of hive-mind-type structure which is working to create scientific conclusions of relevance. Therefore each individual inevitably feels somewhat alienated from the knowledge that they are, perhaps indirectly, involved in producing.</p>\n<p>There&#8217;s a quote that I like, which goes something like: &#8220;It is not enough for the fortunate man to simply be fortunate. He must also believe that he deserves his good fortune. Specifically, that he deserves his good fortune more than the next man.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Your impostor complex is kind of a backwards take on this quote, but it still proves that without belief in your own merit of your good fortune, you are somewhat unsettled.</p>\n<p>Nietzsche argued (to my understanding) that it was possible to become a superman through sheer human will power. That our capabilities are inherently endless, that we can as humans, perhaps within several generations, create the conditions in which we become perfectly suited for any given task of engineering or scholarship. I&#8217;m reaching here, because I haven&#8217;t really read nietzsche, but let&#8217;s let that stand as the idea of the &#8220;superman.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Modern people are inherently obsessed with the idea of the superman. We watch documentaries, CSI Las Vegas, Ultimate Fighting Championship, iron chef, and we get it in our head that people are out there doing great things because they have immense inherent talents and or have undergone unimaginably rigorous training programs. Most biographies of great authors or US presidents are self-consciously burdened with the task of explaining why a person stands out, what makes a given person, a successful person, so different from his peers. Consider the book &#8220;the outliers&#8221; for a good example of this post-facto analysis of exceptionalism.</p>\n<p>So we come away with the idea that there are these incredible people out there, who are inherently super-intelligent, or extremely diligent with the use of their time, or are given extraordinary abilities by their single-mindedness. And when we manage to break in and rub shoulders with the &#8220;elites&#8221; we experience a certain cognitive dissonance caused by the fact that we know that the confused spider&#8217;s web of concepts in our own mind does not resemble the well-tuned scientific process if we are truly among the elites. So either I am a fraud and the people around me understand everything much better than I do, or the people around me are pretending to understand everything but they are just as lost as I am. Or the projected image of who I am supposed to be, as a PhD in astronomy, is inherently unrealistic. Or there are just not that many people who are as diligent as I am about, say, astronomy, even though I play capoeira in the evenings and play video games and go clubbing on the weekends&#8230; even given those faults there are still not enough people who like astronomy and get up early in the morning enough to beat me at my game&#8230;</p>\n<p>So, I will posit to you that by and large the emperor (the academic ivory tower) has no clothes. As a society, we decide to set aside a certain amount of resources (I would probably argue, a pathetically small amount) for the pursuit of knowledge of the universe for its own sake. You have beaten out a high percentage of people in the game of merit that is played for those resources. You are correct in your suspicion that, just because you have proven a certain level of merit, does not mean that you will do anything great. Answering questions on a test goes a long way in securing your financial security and your funding as a scientist (not far enough, you would probably say). But it has very little bearing on whether or not you will actually be instrumental in doing what you have set out to do, which is discover something about the universe. Because the march of science, unlike the march of institutional academia, does not have checkpoints and endpoints pre-built into the system. It is pretty much chaos, everything is up for grabs. There is no merit-based system to determine what you will actually accomplish as a scientist.</p>\n<p>I feel like I&#8217;ve written a lot but said very little. \ud83d\ude42 Kudos to you on your QE&#8217;s.</span></p></blockquote>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-18T02:08:40", "date_gmt": "2012-06-20T02:07:05", "_links": {"wp:term": [{"href": http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/Motivate-self.jpeg?w=411 411w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>When I was studying for the Qualifying Exam, I oftentimes got down about my chances of success. \u00a0So, taking a note from some of the senior graduate students, I started seeking out and writing down words of inspiration. \u00a0While some of them are for me specifically, I figure that the majority of these might work for others as well. So, here they are:</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<p>&#8211; You made it into grad school for a reason. Aside from the slight possibility that you were accepted based on race or personal connections, they would not have admitted you if they didn&#8217;t think that you could handle it and succeed. \u00a0You can do this.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Nick, the difference between who you are and who you want to be is set by what you do in your day-to-day. \u00a0You are working toward your goal diligently and consistently. \u00a0You will reap what you have sown: a carefree summer and a glorious 3rd year.</p>\n<p>&#8211; All of this science was figured out by people. \u00a0<i>People</i>. \u00a0Flawed, occasionally lazy, logical people that somehow found a pathway to understanding. \u00a0For every subject this pathway exists, and since you have the <u>same</u>\u00a0basic hardware that they had when they found the pathway, you can do it too. \u00a0This stuff isn&#8217;t beyond you. \u00a0It might currently be <i>just</i>\u00a0out of reach, but it is not beyond you. \u00a0Stretch the limits of yourself and take hold of what you want. \u00a0It can, and it will, be yours.</p>\n<p>&#8211; You can rise to this challenge, Nick. \u00a0It&#8217;s like Mangang\u00e1 said: Proper Preparation Prevents Passive Poor Performance. \u00a0Actually, let&#8217;s add to that: Proper Preparation, Perseverance, and Patience Prevent Passive Poor Performance.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Keep dad&#8217;s words in your mind at all times: Take the time to do it right. \u00a0Go. \u00a0Go now and fucking do it!</p>\n<p>&#8211; Being defeated is often a temporary condition. \u00a0Giving up is what makes it permanent.</p>\n<p>&#8211; There are no shortcuts to any place worth going.</p>\n<p>&#8211; The pain of discipline is far less than the pain of regret.</p>\n<p>&#8211; When you want to succeed as bad as you want to breathe, then you&#8217;ll be successful (from one of my <a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=lsSC2vx7zFQ\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">favorite videos on YouTube</a>).</p>\n<p>&#8211; Work on your weaknesses until they are strengths, and work on your strengths until they are second nature.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Knowing is not enough, we must apply. \u00a0Willing is not enough, we must do.</p>\n<p>&#8211; It doesn&#8217;t matter when you started. \u00a0It doesn&#8217;t matter how long it took. \u00a0It doesn&#8217;t matter how many times you failed. \u00a0All that matters is that you <i>finished</i>\u00a0what you started.</p>\n<p>&#8211; You <i>must</i>\u00a0do the things you think you can <b>not</b>\u00a0do. \u00a0Keep your head up and your heart strong.</p>\n<p>&#8211; You are in charge of how you feel, and today, choose &#8220;motivated&#8221; and &#8220;accomplished&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8211; Just keep moving forward and don&#8217;t give a shit about what anybody thinks. \u00a0Do what you have to do, for you.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Months of discipline, or a lifetime of unhappiness? \u00a0You pick.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Don&#8217;t let one bad day kill your confidence and progress.</p>\n<p>&#8211; If you like progress, try patience. \u00a0If you like change, try keeping at it.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Strength doesn&#8217;t come from what you<i><b>\u00a0</b>can</i>\u00a0do. \u00a0It comes from overcoming the things you once thought you couldn&#8217;t.</p>\n<p>&#8211; You can feel burnt-out tomorrow or you can feel like a failure come June 13th (the qual date). \u00a0You choose.</p>\n<p>&#8211; If you don&#8217;t want to be\u00a0like everyone else, then you have to be willing to do things differently.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Start by doing what is necessary. \u00a0Then, do what is possible. \u00a0Suddenly, you&#8217;ll find you&#8217;re doing the impossible.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Don&#8217;t be upset with the results you <b>didn&#8217;t get</b>\u00a0from the work that you<b>\u00a0didn&#8217;t do.</b></p>\n<p><b><br />\n</b>&#8211; Most important of all of these: Create a life that you are <i>proud</i>\u00a0to live. \u00a0Do that every day (or work toward it at least), and before you know it you&#8217;ll look back and see a life that you&#8217;re proud to have had.</p>\n<p>&#8211; And one last one, emailed to me by the You Forward"}, "comment_status": "open", "author": 1, "modified_gmt": "2015-09-18T02:06:53", "meta": [], "sticky": false, "slug": "words-to-move-you-forward", "template": "", "ping_status": "open"}, {"featured_media": 68, "format": "standard", "type": "post", "link": "http://rationalwhimsy.com/experiences-with-impostor-syndrome/", "date": "2012-06-19T02:01:46", "content": {"protected": false, "rendered": "<p><img class=\"alignleft size-medium wp-image-68\" src=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/behind-the-mask.jpg?resize=222%2C300\" alt=\"behind-the-mask\" srcset=\"http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/behind-the-mask.jpg?resize=222%2C300 222w, http://i2.wp.com/rationalwhimsy.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/09/behind-the-mask.jpg?w=482 482w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 222px) 100vw, 222px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" />Within the last seven days, my life has encountered two major milestones:</p>\n<p>&#8211; I took the longest, most intense, high-stress exam in my life thus far\u2014the Qualifying Exam.</p>\n<p>&#8211; Two days later, I found out the results of that exam. I passed, apparently without a bit of of debate. \u00a0I officially became Nicholas Hunt-Walker, Master of Astronomy.</p>\n<p>I think the news of my success still has yet to fully hit me. \u00a0Honestly, I have a hard time believing that it&#8217;s actually true\u2014that I managed to pass the qualifying exam at the &#8220;doctoral&#8221; level. \u00a0The first thoughts that come to mind even now are, &#8220;how is this even possible? \u00a0Are they sure that they graded the exam correctly? \u00a0Knowing what I know about astronomy, there&#8217;s no real way in which I could be viable to move forward to the next stage in my graduate career.&#8221;</p>\n<p><!--more--></p>\n<p>It&#8217;s not as if I hadn&#8217;t made a serious attempt to prepare for it. \u00a0Any one of my friends (hell, even my recent ex-girlfriend) could be asked about the extent of my prep work, and they&#8217;d be able to confidently rattle off some double-digit number of hours per week that I had spent working toward this goal. \u00a0There was rarely a day that wasn&#8217;t spent studying, and those that weren&#8217;t were spent working in some capacity so that I&#8217;d have more time later TO STUDY. \u00a0Weekends became cherished not for breaks from work, but for opportunities to study without daily interruptions. \u00a0Evenings became working nights, where I could let the Sun drop below the horizon while I tried to fill my brain to capacity with facts about the universe. \u00a0Meals became brief breathers between pages of my notes. \u00a0The few hours where my mind wasn&#8217;t occupied with thoughts of astronomy (or imminent failure and the consequences of it) could be counted amongst hours of sleep and&#8230;physical recreation (capoeira being one of my principal physical outlets). \u00a0As time went on, even that decompression time became shorter and shorter. \u00a0I say all that to say that, intellectually, I know that I put in the hours.</p>\n<p>However, when I reflect on my knowledge of astronomy and astrophysics, it doesn&#8217;t seem to be sufficient. \u00a0The volume of information that I&#8217;ve packed into my skull seems underwhelming when I think about what it should take in order to qualify as a true Ph.D. candidate. \u00a0Yes I know some things about planets, stars, galaxies, and the universe as a whole. \u00a0I know about some of the astrophysics of the universe, and can tell you any number of things about the mechanics of the universe and the little bits within. \u00a0Hell, even if I don&#8217;t know explicitly what physical laws a particular phenomenon might follow, I can probably make some sensible assumptions about how it might work and derive my way toward something resembling the truth. \u00a0But&#8230;I don&#8217;t know. \u00a0Master of Astronomy? \u00a0It just&#8230;doesn&#8217;t quite seem to fit.</p>\n<p><b><br />\nThis, however, is not a new feeling.</b></p>\n<p>Ever since I had begun taking graduate classes at the University of Washington, I&#8217;ve felt a little out of place. \u00a0This is not because of any deficiency in the astronomy department, I just felt a little behind the pace of my peers locally and nationally. \u00a0My years of intellectual training seemed to fail me, as I wasn&#8217;t always able to calculate or make conceptual connections as quickly as most of my classmates. \u00a0Long and involved derivations seemed to loom before me like dark, foreboding mountains in the distance, insurmountable with whatever I had been equipped with. \u00a0And forget about anything computational. \u00a0Everyone else seemed to see coding instructions as sensible and straight forward, while I always felt like I might as well have been trying to build a fusion reactor with instructions written in Sanskrit (damn you Hydrodynamics!).<br />\nWith these feelings in my gut, as well as the apparently tangible examples of my incompetence (I never did figure out that shock tube&#8230;), I couldn&#8217;t imagine how I&#8217;d make it in this field. \u00a0Never mind all of what I had managed to retain from my classes, and all the moments where my undergraduate training actually served to demonstrate my conceptual understanding. \u00a0Every success was merely me doing what it was that I was supposed to do. \u00a0Every misstep seemed to be the stumbles of someone walking in shoes made for someone else&#8217;s feet.</p>\n<p><b><br />\nI was in a seat meant for someone else.</b></p>\n<p>The <a href=\"http://www.inc.com/magazine/20060901/handson-leadership.html\" data-blogger-escaped-target=\"_blank\">impostor phenomenon</a> is characterized by an individual&#8217;s inability to internalize their accomplishments. \u00a0Instead, the individual feels like any success that they have thus far experienced has been a function of the circumstances, pure luck, pity, or some inexplicable blindness in those evaluating their ability. \u00a0Worse yet, the individual oftentimes feels perpetually fearful of their perceived incompetence being uncovered and that they&#8217;ll be exposed as the fraud that they &#8220;truly&#8221; are. \u00a0Hell, I know that I listened to my advisor&#8217;s voicemail of congratulations at least 5 times to make sure that there wasn&#8217;t anything in her message that I was misunderstanding. \u00a0I had to make sure that the message was real, that she was talking to me, and that she was talking <b>about</b>\u00a0me.</p>\n<p><b><br />\nIn the end, my ability got me into grad school. \u00a0Two years later, that same ability secured my place.</b></p>\n<p><i>It was me</i> sitting for that exam for two sets of 3 hours. \u00a0Sure, my other classmates took theirs at the same time, but <i>it was me</i>\u00a0in front of <i>my</i> exam. \u00a0<i>It was me</i> that answered 10 questions pretty well and two more pretty crappily (is that even a word?). \u00a0<i>I</i> wrote those words on the submitted pages. \u00a0<i>I</i> made the calculations. \u00a0<i>My</i> pseudonym is on the top-left corner of every page, and in the end that proclamation of success came to <i>my</i>\u00a0phone.</p>\n<p>So at least I know that I am where I deserve to be. \u00a0Perhaps, now that I have the time for this sort of thing, I can work toward realizing <i>emotionally</i> that the shoes in which I&#8217;m walking are my own. \u00a0After, maybe I can work on taking bigger steps forward, becoming that model scientist that I know I can be.</p>\n"}, "modified": "2015-09-18T02:04:18", "date_gmt": "2012-06-19T02:01:46",
